M.Sc. Thesis
Master of Science in Engineering
A Comparative Study of a Cogeneration
Plant Repowered with Different Topping
Cycles
- a Gas Turbine Plant and a Solid Oxide Fuel Cell Plant
Ian Isak Kumaraswamy
Kongens Lyngby 2017
==PAGE:2==
DTU Mechanical Engineering
Department of Mechanical Engineering
Technical University of Denmark
Akademivej
Building 403
2800 Kongens Lyngby, Denmark
Phone +45 4525 1960
www.mek.dtu.dk
==PAGE:3==
Abstract
This project thermodynamically investigates, analyses and compares repowering
solutions. The plant to be repowered is a coal fired steam cycle with integrated
seawater desalination using recovered heat from the plant in a direct contact
membrane distillation system.
A gas turbine solution and an SOFC solution
were chosen as repowering topping cycles and both fuelled with natural gas for
comparability.
In order to properly analyse the base case as well as the repowering solutions, they
are designed and modelled in an in-house thermodynamic simulation program -
Dynamic Network Analysis (DNA). They are assessed on several parameters that
repowering can accommodate, such as reducing specific emissions, increasing the
net power production of a plant and gaining higher electric efficiencies, which is
the overall result of the analysis.
The base case steam cycle is initially investigated and it delivers a net power
production of 110.64 MW, a desalinated water production of 74.1 kg/s, recovering
234.7 MJ/s heat for the water production. Furthermore it reaches an electric
efficiency of 29.9%, an energy efficiency of 93.3% and the specific emission of
CO2 amounts to 1.34kgCO2/kW hel.
Three different gas turbine models are initially analysed and compared as repow-
ering solutions. Two Mitsubishi M501DA gas turbine units are chosen including
supplementary firing and recovering additional exhaust heat from the flue-gas for
additional desalination. Integrated with the base case it yields a net power out-
put of 289.4 MW, a water production of 87.7kg/s recovering 277.6 MJ/s heat for
water production. The electric efficiency reaches 43.6% with an energy efficiency
of 85.5% and a specific emission of 0.471 kgCO2/kW hel.
The SOFC topping cycle is evaluated and optimized with a hybrid recuperator,
by sizing the amount of stacks, the fuel utilization factor and modifying operat-
ing temperature. Integrated with the bottoming cycle it produces a net power
production of 586.6 MW, 77.2kg/s potable water from 244.2 MJ/s recovered heat.
Furthermore, the electric efficiency yields 67.2%, the overall energy efficiency,
95.1%, and with a specific emission of 0.304kgCO2/kW hel.
==PAGE:4==
ii
Abstract
The benefits of repowering are an increase in electric efficiency and power output,
and a reduction in specific CO2 emission. Furthermore, repowering with the fuel
cell technology outmatches the conventional gas turbine solution.
From an engineering point of view, repowering, especially with the SOFC tech-
nology, stands as a viable solution to adapt to growing electricity demands while
mitigating CO2 emissions by increasing plant electric efficiencies.

Preface
This Master Thesis was carried out at the department of Mechanical Engineer-
ing at the Technical University of Denmark under the supervision of Professor
Masoud Rokni.
It is the final project fulfilling the requirements for acquiring a Master of Science
in Sustainable Energy. The project amounts to 30 ECTS points and was carried
out from the 1st of June to the 1st of December.

==PAGE:19==
CHAPTER 1
Introduction
1.1
Motivation
The demand for energy on a global scale has not yet reached its limit and with a
growing global population, that point of inflection is still in the future. Further-
more, a transition from energy generation dominated by fossil fuel production
is taking place and a shift towards a more energy efficient production, with fo-
cus on a reduction in CO2 emissions, due to the risk of global warming, is on
the global agenda. The European Commission’s ’Europe 2020 Strategy’ is an
example hereof and with regards to energy it consists of a cut in green house gas,
a demand for more renewable energy and an improvement in energy efficiency.
[Com17] Likewise, the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change
sets a global focus and demand on a transition in the energy sector and mitigat-
ing global warming, with their annual Conference of Parties (COP) and the well
known Paris Agreement.
Large investments are being made in sectors such as wind farms, solar fields
and biomass production, harnessing a more renewable energy profile without
contributing to a green house gas emitting production. These renewable tech-
nologies, however, contribute to a mere fraction of the energy produced - 7.1% of
the world electricity generation is from non-hydro renewables and waste accord-
ing to the IEA’s ’Key World Energy Statistics’. [Age17]
Alongside the shift towards renewable energy, research is likewise urging efficient
use of energy. Repowering thermal power plants by bettering efficiency is a step
towards reducing CO2 emissions.
Steam cycles are, despite the urge to transition towards a more sustainable and
renewable energy portfolio, still commercially dominant. They are responsible
for up to 85% of all global electricity production, because of their suitability for
relatively efficient base load production and the ability to be fuelled on a wide
array of fuels. [Tur17]
Many old thermal power plants are still in operation running on fossil fuels with
low electric efficiencies. Instead of decommissioning power plants that have a
==PAGE:20==
2
1 Introduction
considerable life expectancy, repowering these power plants could increase their
energy efficiency, produce more power to adapt to growing demands and mitigate
their contribution to global warming risk by reducing the specific CO2 emissions.
In this way repowering steam cycles with a topping cycle which leads to better
electric efficiencies and a reduction in green house gases, does not replace carbon
capture storages related to fossil fuel generated power. Instead it minimizes the
cost of CO2 avoided, and bridges the gap between an energy producing portfolio
dominated by thermal power plants with long life expectancy and large invest-
ment in carbon neutral technology or renewables. [ER09]
Designing repowering schemes for thermal power plants are traditionally seen
with a gas turbines integrated as topping cycles with a steam cycle. This concept,
equivalent to a combined cycle setup, can be challenged by introducing potentially
new large power producing technologies such as the fuel cell - specifically the
solid oxide fuel cell. Introducing topping cycles with higher electric efficiencies,
is expected to yield higher overall plant electric efficiencies, when integrated with
a bottoming cycle.
Alongside electricity production from thermal power plants, there is an option
of recovering potentially large amounts of heat. This heat can be utilized differ-
ently, however an option is introducing the concept of desalinating seawater in
repowering cases.
Approximately a quarter of the world’s population stands without access to fresh
water and 47 % of the world’s population will by 2030 be living in areas of ”high
water stress”.
[OEC14] Seawater makes up approximately 97 % of the water
reserves. With the concept of desalination and fresh water production, the earth’s
water reserves have a large potential for accompanying areas of ”high water stress”
especially in dry countries surrounded by seawater. A growing demand for power
is seen concurrently with a growing demand for fresh potable water. Hence a
dual-purpose plant, with power and fresh water production is ideal.[PAZ15]
1.2
Objective
This paper aims to analyse and compare, from a thermodynamic perspective,
repowering solutions for a steam cycle including a desalination system for utilizing
recovered heat.
Initially a coal fired steam cycle with a desalination system is defined, designed
and analysed - this base case that will be repowered.
The conventional repowering scheme, with a natural gas fired single or set of
gas turbines along with supplementary firing is then considered. A selection of
==PAGE:21==
1.2 Objective
3
three different gas turbine models are analysed and compared in setups of 1-3
gas turbine units consisting of a topping cycle including supplementary firing if
necessary.
To challenge the conventional repowering scheme, the solid oxide fuel cell is in-
troduced and designed to fit the bottoming cycle. It will be discussed in terms of
design regarding heat exchangers, and optimized on sizing the number of stacks,
investigating the fuel utilization factor and modifying the operation temperature.
For comparability purposes natural gas is used as the fuel source for the fuel cell.
In order to properly assess the base case as well as the different repowering
schemes, each option is carefully designed and analysed by carrying out thermo-
dynamic simulations in the in-house simulation tool - Dynamic Network Analysis.
==PAGE:22==
4
==PAGE:23==
CHAPTER 2
Technology Overview
This chapter will briefly introduce the different main technologies that are studied
in this paper. First, the steam cycle power plant, which is the technology used in
the base case, will be introduced along with the concept of seawater desalination
and the chosen technology for it. Finally, the technologies for the repowering so-
lutions are introduced - the gas turbine cycle and the fuel cell concept, specifically
the SOFC.
• Steam cycle
• Desalination
• Gas Turbine
• Solid Oxide Fuel Cell
2.1
Energy Demand, Energy Shift and Repowering
The demand for energy on a global scale has not yet reached its limit. On the
contrary, with a growing global population and a strive for constant development,
that point of inflection is still in the future. Electricity plays a major role and
is a demanded resource when it comes to development. Figure 2.1 is taken from
’The Shift Project Data Portal’ and shows the global electricity production by
source of energy involved in generating it.[Por14] As one may notice, coal is the
largest contributor, gas and nuclear power are also large contributors.
As mentioned, a transition from a primarily fossil fuel based electricity production
towards a more renewable energy production, with a focus on a reduction in CO2
emissions, due to the risk of global warming, is on the global agenda. This energy
shift is under political development. A shift to invest in and generate power from
larger shares of renewable energy sources is taking place in global policy making
today. The European Commission’s ’Europe 2020 Strategy’ is an example hereof
and with regards to energy it consists of a cut in green house gas, a demand for
==PAGE:24==
6
2 Technology Overview
Figure 2.1: World electricity production from all energy sources, from [Por14]
more renewable energy and an improvement in energy efficiency. [Com17] This
is not only adherent in a supranational context - individual countries or states
pushing the agenda. Likewise, the United Nations Framework Convention on
Climate Change sets a global focus and demand on a transition in the energy
sector and mitigating global warming, with their annual Conference of Parties
(COP) and the well known Paris Agreement.
With society demanding transition towards a more sustainable and renewable
energy portfolio, the concept of repowering can be a bridging solution between a
majority of thermal power plants that remain dominant within power production
on a global scale.
The steam cycle contributes to approximately 85% of the global electricity pro-
duction and coal is the primary fuel source along with nuclear, gas and oil to
some extent.[Tur17]
A large part of the Rankine based cycles, have due to aging shown a decrease in
electric efficiency. Furthermore, they are fuelled primarily on coal or other fossil
fuels. To comply with the on-going energy shift, repowering such power plants
could increase their output and electric efficiency and reduce their CO2 emissions
and thereby reduce their contribution to global warming risk.
An important parameter in repowering applications is the expected life of equip-
ment. Often old thermal power plants that show a decrease in efficiency still have
a long life expectancy. Instead of decommissioning these plants prematurely, a
more cost efficient approach is considering repowering. Hence, repowering ther-
mal power plants, with a properly selected/designed topping cycle, can be a
time-efficient and cost-efficienct way of increasing capacity, reaching better elec-
tric efficiencies and reducing CO2 emissions per unit installed capacity.
==PAGE:25==
2.1 Energy Demand, Energy Shift and Repowering
7
Repowering can be defined as increasing the capacity of an existing thermal
power plant, while increasing the electric efficiency and decreasing the emissions
per installed capacity.
A hurdle for repowering versus decommissioning and erecting new power plants,
is that the repowered power plant should have a lower levelized cost of electricity
(LCOE) than the former, and it should overall be competitive with new solutions.
2.1.1
Repowering Concepts
Traditionally repowering is done with a gas turbine topping cycle. The main
repowering concepts using gas turbines in commercial use are
• full repowering, also known as heat recovery repowering or boiler repowering
• site repowering
• feed water heating
• hot windbox repowering
Full repowering is when one or several gas turbines are installed as a topping cycle
and the previous boiler in the steam cycle is replaced with a heat recovery steam
generator (HRSG). Live steam from the steam cycle is heated in the HRSG by
gas turbine exhaust gas. This is the type of repowering that garners the largest
improvements in efficiency and output. The repowered plant electric efficiency
can improve by 30-40% and net power output can increase by at least 150-200%
depending on the amount of GTs included. [ER09]
Site repowering is in the sense erecting a new combined cycle power plant us-
ing the previous site, and hence isn’t considered further, because most of the
components and equipment are replaced. [Gül15]
Feed water repowering is a method where hot exhaust gases, from a gas turbine
or another source such as solar power, heats the feedwater. Extraction points
from the steam turbine are removed - the turbine doesn’t bleed steam - and
hence more steam will be diverted to the low pressure area. This process requires
modification of low pressure section of the plant. Using feed water repowering can
alter the power output by approximately a 30-40% increase and electric efficiency
increase of about 5-10%. [ER09]
Hot windbox repowering is a seperate method, and it can be implemented in
several ways. Firstly, it requires a redesign of the power plant. The main concept
is that the hot exhaust gases from a gas turbine is fed into the original boiler
==PAGE:26==
8
2 Technology Overview
and used as a comburent. Due to the content of O2 it can combust or aid in
combusting the coal.
A slightly different hot windbox method is by diluting the exhaust gas with
fresh air, hereby adding O2 to the gas and decreasing the temperature of the
combustion gases. [ER09]
Whatever choice of repowering that is taken on will leave the engineer or designer
with decisions regarding deciding to optimize the existing steam turbine versus
installing a new one. Also whether to maintain the same strategy with regards to
steam bleeding or pursue a new one, which will lead to a modification in the feed
water heaters and components in the low pressure section. Deciding whether the
goal is to maximize the steam turbine output or reduce cost by avoiding redesign.
For the repowered plant to be competitive with a new power plant, it is important
to consider optimization of the bottoming cycle. An increased capital investment
may be justifiable if the net output and electric efficiency is garner large enough
increase. [Gül15]
In this project, the full repowering concept is considered replacing the coal fired
boiler with an HRSG. Apart from that the bottoming cycle is maintained as prior
to repowering, for simplicity. The topping cycles analysed are combinations of
gas turbines with supplementary firing and an optimization of a solid oxide fuel
cell.
2.2
Steam Cycle
The steam cycle power plant is among the most dominant technologies within
power production with regards to installed capacity. As mentioned, the steam cy-
cle contributes to approximately 85% of the global electricity production.[Tur17]
It has been historically dominant due to a wide array of applicable fuels - espe-
cially large quantities of coal, high electric efficiencies, large unit capacities and
the early technology advancement of the steam engine. [Nag08]
2.2.1
Principle of the Rankine Cycle
The steam cycle or Rankine cycle is a thermodynamic cycle based on compression,
heat addition, expansion and condensation, where the working fluid is steam. The
steam cycle is primarily fuelled with coal, gas, nuclear power or oil. Biomass and
solar heat (concentrated solar power) have also been introduced as fuels for the
steam cycle in recent years. Furthermore, low grade energy such as waste heat can
be used to fuel organic rankine cycles (ORC), however this type of Rankine cycle
uses a working fluid that has a lower boiling temperature than water.[Nag08]
==PAGE:27==
2.2 Steam Cycle
9
A schema of the simple ideal Rankine cycle is seen in figure 2.2 taken from
[PAZ15]. It consists of a pump, a boiler, a turbine and a condenser - with steam
as the working fluid. The steam cycle itself is a closed loop. The thermodynamic
principle is seen in the T-s diagram (temperature-entropy) of the cycle in figure
2.3.
The basic principle of the simple ideal Rankine cycle is, as one can follow in 2.2
and thermodynamically in figure 2.3, that water is pumped to a high pressure,
then heat is added in the boiler and a phase change of the working fluid occurs
from water to steam in the boiler. Depending on the design, the working fluid
is, apart from being evaporated, usually superheated in the boiler to ensure no
phase change in the turbine and thereby avoid water droplets on the turbine
blades during the expansion. The superheated steam is as mentioned expanded
over the turbine to a low pressure, which makes the turbine rotate and generate
power. Finally the low pressure steam is condensed in a condenser changing its
phase back to saturated water. [Hag14] [Nag08]
Figure 2.2: A schema of a simple ideal
Rankine cycle, [PAZ15]
Figure 2.3: A T-s diagram of a simple
ideal Rankine cycle, [PAZ15]
The ideal Rankine cycle is described in theory, because the real cycle would have
a lower efficiency. The thermal efficiency is defined as the ratio between the net
work produced and the total heat delivered. The simple ideal Rankine cycle can
achieve electric efficiencies up to approximately 43 % and the simple real steam
cycle reaches electric efficiencies around 36 %. [CB07]
The simple Rankine cycle can be modified to reach higher efficiencies. Based
on the principle of the Carnot cycle, efficiency is increased by either increasing
the temperature transferred to the working fluid or decreasing the temperature
rejected from the working fluid.
==PAGE:28==
10
2 Technology Overview
Reheat can be applied, which is a concept where the steam is extracted from the
expansion process at an earlier stage, led back to the boiler and reheated and
returned to the turbine to finish the expansion. A diagram of a Rankine cycle
modified with reheat can be seen in figure 2.4 from [PAZ15]. By applying reheat
to the Rankine cycle, the reheated Rankine cycle can increase electric efficiencies
by approximately 4-5 %. [CB07]
Furthermore, the concept of regeneration is another proposed alternative for in-
creasing the efficiency of the Rankine cycle. In regeneration, a fraction of steam
is extracted from the turbine to heat up the feedwater prior to entering the boiler.
A diagram of a Rankine cycle modified with regeneration can be seen in figure 2.5
taken from [PAZ15]. By implementing regeneration in the Rankine cycle, electric
efficiencies of about 38-40 % can be acheived. [CB07]
Figure 2.4: A Rankine cycle modified
with reheat, [PAZ15]
Figure 2.5: A Rankine cycle modified
with regeneration, [PAZ15]
The steam cycle can also be modified by combining it with another power plant -
combined cycle power plants. The more dominating combination is a gas turbine
topping cycle, however alternative solutions to combining cycles such as using
fuel cells, solar power, heat engines etc. are also seen. Repowering steam cycle
power plants can be done by implementing a combined cycle approach. [Rok15a]
[Rok15f]
Finally, the steam cycle differentiates between condensing turbines and back-
pressure turbines.
This distinction is based on the expanded steam pressure.
Condensing plants expand steam to pressures below atmospheric pressure and
after expansion it condenses the steam - they receive a higher power output the
larger the expansion. In the back-pressure mode, the steam pressure expansion
is stopped earlier, at higher pressures and temperatures, and they produce less
power than the condensing turbines, however the expanded back-pressure steam
==PAGE:29==
2.3 Desalination of Seawater
11
temperature is high enough to use for either district heating or process heat and
therefore the overall efficiency of the plant is usually higher than for condensing
turbines.[Hag14]
2.2.2
Asnæs Power Plant
In order to study a steam cycle power plant the Asnæs Power Plant block 2 is
chosen and a slightly modified version of it is used as a base case. The general
values and BoP are seen in Appendix A as well as in table 3.1.
The Asnæs Power Plant is located in Kalundborg, Denmark and it is a coal pow-
ered CHP plant, with two active blocks, block 2 and 5. Block 5 is the largest
single power plant block with a net power capacity of 640 MW at condensing
mode and 308 MJ/s heat production at backpressure mode. Block 2 has a net
power output of 142MW at condensing mode and 193MJ/s heat at backpressure
mode. Only one block generates power at a time and they produce power and
heat to Novo Nordisk, Novozymes and the local district heating grid.[Ene17]
Furthermore, the plant will undergo a transition to a more renewable and sus-
tainable energy source, because biomass will be introduced to the plant and this
new block will produce 25MW power and up to 129MJ/s heat. [Ene17]
In this study, however, only block 2 of the Asnæs Power Plant will be used as a
reference case. As mentioned, a simplified and modified version of the block will
be used as the base case for the steam power plant and only the power output
of 142 MW will be used as the target value for designing the system along with
the available input settings gathered from Appendix A and mentioned in the
methods.
2.3
Desalination of Seawater
Desalination is a process that removes or lowers the salinity of a substance. In
general it refers to a process that removes an amount of salt (and other minerals)
from a given saline substance, separating it into two streams; a distillate (no
salts) or permeate(low dissolved salt content) and a brine (concentrated saline
liquid). The process of desalination is ideal producing potable water from sea
water, but it is also seen in soil desalination, or the production of concentrates.
[IAE15]
According to a report by the OECD, approximately 25% of the world’s population
stands without access to fresh water and 97.6% of the water reserves on earth are
in the form of seawater. In this lies a large potential for fresh water production
==PAGE:30==
12
2 Technology Overview
using the concept of desalination.[OEC14] Desalination is considered a good so-
lution especially in dry countries surrounded by seawater, specifically countries
in the Arabian Peninsula and in general the Middle East and the Meditteranean
region. [PAZ15]
The concept of desalination, if implemented on a large scale, requires energy in
the form thermal or mechanical energy predominantly. Integrating thermal power
plants and desalination system, recovering heat for the desalination process, is
seen in several examples, especially in the Middle East.[Wat14][Wat15] There is a
growing demand for dual purpose integrated power plants, such as cogeneration,
with regards to seawater desalination, due to rapidly evolving technologies within
the field. [IAE15]
2.3.1
Types of Desalination
In scientific discourse, the process of desalination is often classified in two main
categories: thermal processes and membrane processes. Thermal processes are
when feedwater is boiled and vapour is condensed as potable water and they
include eg.
multi-stage flash, multi-effect distillation and vapour compression
among others.
Membrane processes are when saline water is filtered using a
membrane and they include eg. reverse osmosis, electrodialysis and membrane
distillation.
Both processes are endothermic, ie. they require energy, and depending on the
process type chosen, the energy required varies, also in form be it electricity,
thermal energy, mechanical power or chemical energy.[PAZ15]
The desalination processes are classified in table 2.1, taken from [VUS15], by
energy type and the physical process involved.
The three most important and commercially dominant industrial technologies for
desalination are:
• MSF - Multi-Stage Flash
• MED - Multi-Effect Distillation
• RO - Reverse Osmosis
Pankratz (2014) discusses the dominance of the main technologies for seawater
desalination purposes, which can be seen in figure 2.6, and they make up 93%
of seawater desalination processes installed worldwide. This dominance is due
to cost, but also capacities of the three dominant technologies - the ability to
produce potable water from seawater in large scale production.[Pan14]
==PAGE:31==
2.3 Desalination of Seawater
13
Table 2.1: Desalination Process Classification from [VUS15]
Energy
Process
Method
Thermal
Evaporation
Multi-stage flash (MSF)
Multi-effect distillation (MED)

Solar still
Crystallisation
Freezing

Filtration and Evaporation

Mechanical
Evaporation
Mechanical vapour compression
Filtration
Reverse Osmosis (RO)
Electrical
Selective filtration
Electrodialysis (ED)
Chemical
Exchange
Ionic Exchange (IE)
Reverse osmosis is a membrane separation process that uses filtration. The mem-
brane in use is a microporous hydrophobic type, only permeable to water vapour
and not liquid. Hence, separated by this semi-permeable membrane, a concen-
trated solute is applied high pressures which pushes the solvent through the
membrane and leaves back highly concentrated solute and only solvent, water
vapour molecules that are small enough pass through.[PAZ15]
In the multi-stage flash process seawater is heated in a heater and flows into a
stage where the ambient pressure is low enough to ensure that the boiling point
is lower than the seawater is heated to, hence making the seawater boil instantly,
where the phase change is described as a flash. Only a small fraction is boiled,
and the remaining saline water passes to the next stage where the pressure is
even lower creating the same instant boiling. A MSF plant typically has between
4-40 stages. In each stage, the steam is condensed to water using cooling tubes,
cooled by the incoming seawater.[IAE15]
The multi-effect distillation technology is also a thermal process. Preheated sea-
water enters a chamber where a steam loop, heated by steam from a heat source,
exchanges heat to the seawater and it is raised to boiling point. A portion of
the seawater is evaporated and the vapour continues through a tube to the next
chamber. The brine enters the chamber as well but not in the tube, and therefore
it condenses the vapour, and the energy exchange vapourizes some of the brine
and the two continue to the next chamber, which continues in the scale of 16-80
stages. [IAE15]
RO has in later decades been the more dominating technology with approximately
2/3 and MSF with approximately 1/4 of the installed capacity as seen in figure 2.6.
==PAGE:32==
14
2 Technology Overview
Figure 2.6: The total worldwide installed capacity by technology [Pan14]
This picture is however constantly changing, due to technological advances.
Some of the areas that challenge the desalination technologies are eg.
costs,
working temperatures, high pressure gradients, corrosion, heat exchanger scaling,
tube failure and fouling. Developments in design and construction, operation
and control have all created a path for better performance ratios (PR), higher
gain output ratios (GOR) and lower production costs as well as specific energy
consumption.
2.3.2
Membrane Distillation
Membrane distillation is a thermally driven alternative to the conventional seper-
ation processes such as reverse osmosis. [LL97] In this case also a hydrophobic
membrane separates solutes from solvents by the microporous size of the holes in
the membrane allowing only vapour to pass. The process is thermally driven in
the sense that the temperature gradient between the hot and cold side of the mem-
brane causes a pressure difference over the membrane and the vapour molecules
are due to the pressure difference transported through the membrane. Basically
an evaporation of vapour occurs and the transport of the vapour is driven by
a pressure difference due to a temperature difference. Usually the seawater is
==PAGE:33==
2.3 Desalination of Seawater
15
heated and only solvent or permeate remains on the other side of the membrane
at a lower temperature. When the water vapour is driven through the membrane
it condenses on the other side due to the cooler environment.
Membrane distillation can operate at atmospheric pressures and temperatures
below 100◦C.
The membrane distillation concept consists of four different types
• DCMD - Direct Contact Membrane Distillation
• AGMD - Air Gap Membrane Distillation
• SGMD - Sweeping Gas Membrane Distillation
• VMD - Vacuum Membrane Distillation
The direct contact membrane distillation is the simplest of the four MD config-
urations and the principle is seen in figure 2.7 from [ADH12]. In the DCMD
configuration, the hot feed water (seawater or fluid that needs to be filtered) is in
direct contact with the hot side of the membrane surface. The feed water cannot
penetrate the membrane, due to proper hydrophobic membrane characteristics,
and the feed water requires to be evaporated to vapour. This happens at mem-
brane surface hot side. The water vapour molecule is then driven through the
membrane by a pressure difference caused by the temperature difference between
heated feed water and cooled permeate.
DCMD is seen in commercial desalination processes. A drawback of the DCMD
configuration is that heat is lost by conduction. [ADH12]
Figure 2.7: The direct contact membrane configuration [ADH12]
To tackle the conduction losses, the air gap membrane distillation configuration
is introduced. Again the feed is in contact with the hot side of the membrane.
==PAGE:34==
16
2 Technology Overview
However, air is introduced between the membrane and the cold surface on the
permeate side. With an air gap, the heat lost by conduction is reduced. Other
disadvantages occur however, such as resistances to mass transfer. [ADH12]
Like the air gap design, the sweeping gas membrane configuration uses a gas to
sweep the permeate to condense outside the membrane. This however requires
large condening areas for a viable production of permeate.
In the vacuum membrane distillation configuration a pump produces a vacuum
on the permeate side of the membrane, and condensing takes place outside the
membrane and conduction losses are negligible. [ADH12]
2.3.3
Comparing Technologies
Litterature on the different seawater desalination technologies doesn’t necessarily
agree regarding which technologies are from an engineering stand point the more
feasibly and viably superior approach.
In general, thermal desalination processes consume more energy, 10-15kWh/m3,
compared to reverse osmosis at approximately 5 kWh/m3. However, due to reli-
ability and large scale production they remain competitive.[IAE15]
The RO process’ membranes are sensitive to scaling, fouling and thereby mem-
brane degradation, which makes RO dependent on pretreatment of the seawater.
[AK11]
Recent research by Ophier and Lokiec (2005) favours MED to the other domi-
nant technologies due to use of inexpensive material. Water costs were found
down to 0.54 $/m3. [OL05] Likewise Gunzbourg and Larger (1999) discusses the
relevance of applying high efficiency MED desalination plants with cogeneration
and thereby reducing costs for potable water, making this thermal desalination
technology competetive to RO. The estimated cost of desalinated water from
an MED unit was found under reasonable assumptions to be less than 0.5$/m3.
Gunzbourg and Larger (1999) argue that it is due to MED being cheaper, more
flexible, consuming less electricity and allowing for higher efficiency than the MSF
technology. [GL99]
Membranes are prone to less fouling due to larger pore sizes compared to other
distillation technologies such as RO. With less fouling comes less demand for mem-
brane characteristics and hence less expensive materials for the membrane can
be applied, such as plastic and thereby avoiding issues with corrosion. [ADH12]
Inexpensive materials lead to inexpensive capital costs, which make the MD pro-
cess attractive. Obaidani et al. considers the DCMD process in particular to
be competitive with other thermally driven processes and using waste heat, even
competitive with reverson osmosis. [AlO+08] Furthermore, the low working tem-
==PAGE:35==
2.4 Gas Turbine
17
peratures and especially low pressures make the MD process competitive. MD
can desalinate both seawater and brackish water. All in all the MD technology
is from a research persective deemed a cost effective separation process able to
utilize low temperature heat sources or even alternative energy sources. However,
from a commercial perspective, little attention and acceptance has been given to
the MD technology and hence it only little experience in large-scale industrial
applications.[ElB+06]
The DCMD configuration of the membrane distillation technology is applied for
desalination purposes in this project and will be applied on a large scale setup.
2.3.4
Desalination Integrated with Steam Cycle
A growing demand for power is seen concurrently with a growing demand for fresh
potable water. Hence a cogeneration plant, also called a dual-purpose plant, with
power and fresh water production is an ideal solution.[PAZ15]
Combining power plants and desalination systems is attracting more attention
due to the better thermodynamic efficiencies and economic optimization in these
dual purpose plants. [IAE15] The solution for optimizing fuel utilization in a
power plant by producing power and fresh water is slowly being encouraged in
warmer and dryer geographical areas over the conventional CHP plants seen in
Northern Europe.
CHP plants in Northern Europe generate power and heat,
using the heat for district heating purposes or process heat for industrial appli-
cations.
Different combinations of power generation and desalination are seen commis-
sioned around the world.
Large plants using gas turbines with a hybrid con-
figuration of different desalination technologies such as MSF and RO combined.
[Wat15] [Wat14] Also steam cycle plants running in back-pressure mode using
MSF, MED, RO or a hybrid configuration for desalination purposes.[ElN01] Even
concepts using waste heat, solar heat and geothermal energy are attracting at-
tention. [ElB+06]
2.4
Gas Turbine
The gas turbine is applicable for power plant purposes and is based on the Brayton
Cycle. Furthermore, it is seen in relation to aircrafts in the shape of turbojets,
turbofans or turboshafts.
It differs to the steam turbine in the way that the working fluid, which consists of
fuel and compressed air, mixes, combusts to an exhaust gas and expands over the
==PAGE:36==
18
2 Technology Overview
turbine. The steam cycle keeps the fuel that heats the boiler seperate from the
steam loop - the working fluid. A simple ideal Brayton cycle will be explained.
As seen in figure 2.8 the gas turbine consists of a compressor, a burner and a
turbine. Following the T-s diagram, air is compressed isentropically from 1-2,
then fuel is added in the burner and combustion occurs without heat losses in
the components in 2-3. Finally the hot high pressure flue-gases are expanded
isentropcially over the turbine until ambient pressure from 3-4. [Sar08]
Figure 2.8: The configuration and a T-s diagram of a simple ideal Brayton cycle
[Sar08]
The simple ideal Brayton cycle can be optimized in different ways. The three
main methods of optimization are regeneration (recuperation) also called the
heat exchange cycle, intercooled compression and reheat expansion. The combi-
nation of all three optimizations are called the “complex Brayton cycle”. [Tya+06]
[Rok15d]
Regeneration or recuperation is an optimization where the hot exhaust gases are
regenerated in a heat exchanger (regenerator) to heat up the compressed air prior
to entering the combustion chamber. This optimizes the cycle, because less fuel
is necessary in the combustion, due to added heat from the regenerator. Hence,
it optimizes the efficiency of the cycle. Intercooled compression is an optimiza-
tion where the compression process is split in two or several steps. Compression
increases the pressure but also the temperature, and between each compression
step, the partially compressed air is cooled in order to reach a higher compres-
sion ratio. The intercooling is a power output optimization. Finally, reheat is an
optimization, similar to that of the reheat process in steam cycles. The turbine
==PAGE:37==
2.4 Gas Turbine
19
expansion process is split and the working fluid is reheated in another combustion
chamber. The reheat optimization is a power output optimization at the cost of
efficiency. [RSR15] [Tya+06]
The three optimizations can be seen in the T-s diagram in figure 2.9 from [RSR15].
Intercooling is seen from 2-3, regeneration or recuperation is seen stretching the
diagram from 4R-5 and 8-8R, and finally reheat is seen from 6-7. The numbering
containing an s, resembles an isentropic compression or expansion and is only
relevant in a reversible adiabatic process.
Figure 2.9: The configuration and a T-s diagram of a complex Brayton cycle
[RSR15]
Besides the simple gas turbine and optimized ”complex Brayton cycle”, the gas
turbine can be designed in other configurations. The Steam Injection Gas Turbine
(STIG) injects a fraction of steam into the burner (often water heated by a
recuperator using recovered heat from the exhaust gas) which has the effect of
reducing NOx gases and increasing power output.
Similarly the Evaporative Gas Turbine (EvGT) uses water for intercooling, and
this water is then evaporated by the hot exhaust gases in a recuperator, after
which it enters the combustion chamber.
It has a positive effect on the net
power output by intercooling and by increasing the mass flow through the turbine.
[Hor98]
The Indirect Fuel Gas Turbine (IFGT) and similarly the Wet Indirect Fuel Gas
Turbine is fuelled in a combustion chamber, where the combusted gas doesn’t
==PAGE:38==
20
2 Technology Overview
enter the turbomachinery. Instead, the flue-gases from the combustion chamber
heats compressed air in a heat exchanger, and hence dirty fuels or fuels with large
particles and even wet fuels can be used. [Rok15e]
Finally the gas turbine cycle is an ideal solution for combined cycle power plants
(CCPP). It is a dominant technology regarding topping cycles for combined cycle
power plants due to the hot exhaust gases exiting the turbine. A schema and
T-s diagram of a combined cycle power plant using a gas turbine and a steam
cycle is seen in figure 2.10 and figure 2.11 from [PAZ15]. In this case, one may
notice that a simple gas turbine cycle is used. Regenerative optimization is not
necessarily ideal in a combined cycle, because the optimization recovers energy
from the exhaust gases which is where the bottoming cycle should recover energy
from. [Rok15a]
Figure 2.10: GT cycle and steam cycle
[PAZ15]
Figure 2.11: A T-s diagram of a GT and
steam cycle [PAZ15]
The gas turbine is used in combined cycle power plants as a topping cycle as seen
in figure 2.10. Similarly, a GT can be used as a topping cycle for repowering a
steam cycle. This method will be modelled and analysed further.
2.5
Solid Oxide Fuel Cell
The fuel cell technology is an electrochemical energy conversion, meaning it can
convert chemical energy, in the shape of gaseuos fuel, into electricity and heat as
a byproduct.
==PAGE:39==
2.5 Solid Oxide Fuel Cell
21
2.5.1
Working Principle of the Fuel Cell
The fuel cell consists of two electrodes and a seperating layer between - the
electrolyte.
• Anode - the positive electrode
• Electrolyte - the layer or membrane that seperates the electrodes and allows
only the flow of ions
• Cathode - the negative electrode
Fuel is supplied to the anode and an oxidant to the cathode. The fuel and the
oxidant react in the electrolyte. Depending on the type of electrolyte, either only
positive ions can move through from the anode to the cathode, or negative ions
can move from the cathode to the anode. The electrons that are freed up then
return to the other side of the electrolyte creating an electric circuit. [Rok15c]
Figure 2.12: The principle of the PEM technology
The basic principle of the PEM technology is seen in figure 2.12 from [LD03] and
the chemical reactions are seen in equation 2.1, 2.2 and 2.3. Hydrogen is fuelled
to the cell in the anode (2H2) and reacts chemically so that the positive hydrogen
ions (4H+) are seperated from the electrons (4e−). The positive hydrogen ions
pass through the electrolyte (a specific electrolyte is chosen that only allows
positive ions to travel from anode to cathode) and react with the oxidant, oxygen
(O2), and the electrons that were realeased from the anode that were passed
around the external circuit. When the oxygen, electrons and positive hydrogen
ions react, the released energy, ∆G, along with the emissions, water (2H2O), are
the product of the reactions. [LD03]
2H2 ⇒4H+ + 4e−
(2.1)
==PAGE:40==
22
2 Technology Overview
02 + 4e−+ 4H+ ⇒2H2O
(2.2)
2H2 + O2 ⇒2H2O + ∆G
(2.3)
In equation 2.4, ∆G is the change in Gibbs free energy, which is defined as the
maximum reversible non-espansion work extractable.
∆G = −n · F · E
(2.4)
E is the reversible potential of the cell, F is the Faraday constant and n is the
number of electrons in the reaction.
There are however several losses involved in the irriversible fuel cell. They can be
seen in figure 2.13 ([EG04]) and are a sum of concentration losses, ohmic losses
and activations losses. Activation losses occur due to the activation energy of
chemical reactions and are seen in the first large decline of the slope. Ohmic losses
are in essence resistences in the electrolyte, anode and cathode and are seen after
the activation losses with a smaller slope and more linear slope. Concentration
losses occur due to transport limitations and are seen in the final drop of the
voltage curve. [EG04]
Figure 2.13: Concentration, ohmic, activation losses displayed with the voltage
drop as a function of the current
==PAGE:41==
2.5 Solid Oxide Fuel Cell
23
The losses can also be shown as in equation 2.5 as the fuel cell potential or
operational voltage, EF C, as a sum of the ideal cell potential or Nernst reversible
voltage, ENernst, minus the activation, ohmic and concentration losses. [Rok12]
[Rok15c]
EF C = ENernst −∆Eact −∆Eohm −∆Econc
(2.5)
In order to avoid too large losses, a significant amount of fuel is passed through
the fuel cell, and in fact more fuel than converted is sent to the fuel cell. This
can be described as the fuel utilization factor and is seen in equation 2.6.
Uf = mass of fuel reacted
mass of fuel input
(2.6)
2.5.2
Fuel Cell Classification
There is a wide array of fuel cell technologies commercially available and each
fuel cell type has its advantages and disadvantages such as size, affordability,
durability, efficiency, able to work under dynamic conditions etc.
Low temperature FCs (50◦C - 250◦C)
• Alcaline Fuel Cell (AFC)
• Proton Exchange Membrane Fuel Cell (PEM or PEMFC)
• Phosphoric Acid Fuel Cell (PAFC)
High temperature FCs (650◦C - 1000◦C)
• Direct Methanol Fuel Cell (DMFC)
• Molten Carbonate Fuel Cell (MCFC)
• Solid Oxide Fuel Cell (SOFC)
As seen above, the fuel cells can be classified based on their operating temperature.
Certain litterature also classifies fuel cells based on the electrolyte used.
The DMFC fuel cell is primarily for low range appliances such as portable elec-
tronics, between 1 and 100 W. The PEMFC and AFC fuel cell types ranges
between 1kW and 10kW and are seen in cars, boats, transportation in general
==PAGE:42==
24
2 Technology Overview
and small scale CHPs. The PAFC, SOFC and MCFC are cell types more suit-
able for large scale power producing in ranges counting several kWs and up to
many MWs and are often seen in power generation and CHP plants. Common for
the low temperature fuel cells are that their efficiency is in the range of 40-50%,
whereas the high temperature FCs can have efficiencies around 60%. [KJN09]
Hydrogen, H2, is the primary fuel type used in fuel cells, however fuel cells in
general have the ability to use different types of fuels, making the fuel cell ad-
vantageous to other electricity producing technologies. Other types of fuels used
in fuel cells are natural gas (see table 3.9 for an NG composition), methanol
(CH3OH), ethanol (C2H5OH), ammonia (NH3), syngas from gasification pro-
cesses such as biomass gasification or domestic waste gasification, and Di-Methyl
Ether (DME CH3OCH3).[Rok15c]
Figure 2.14: The efficiency of the fuel cell compared to that of the Carnot Cycle
of conventional technology
An advantage of the fuel cell is that it in general is more efficient than other power
producing technologies, be it combustion engines or turbomachinery as seen in
figure 2.14. The fuel cell efficiency depends on the fuel type. As seen in figure
2.14 using H2 as fuel, the fuel cell is more efficient than conventional cycles as
a function of the operating temperature until approximately 700◦C, after that
conventional technology can outmatch the fuel cell. If methane is used as fuel
in the fuel cell, the fuel is more efficient and the conventional technologies can
only compete when operating temperatures increase to more than 1100◦C, which
beyond the scale of this figure.[Rok15c]
==PAGE:43==
2.5 Solid Oxide Fuel Cell
25
The fuel cell is also essentially simple, in the sense that it is built up of few moving
parts. Hence leading to reliability and endurance. With regards to emissions, the
fuel cell has the possibility, depending on the chosen fuel, to emit no greenhouse
gasses (GHG) or ozone-depleting substances (ODS). If hydrogen is used as a fuel
the process would emit water. However, one should be critical when labelling fuel
cells as ’zero emission’ technologies, because the fuel itself may include refining
processes that emit GHGs or ODSs. Furthermore, the fuel cells are relatively
quiet compared to other power producing technologies, having an effect on exter-
nalities - surroundings. The major disadvantage of the fuel cell technology is still
the cost. [LD03]
2.5.3
SOFC
The SOFC (Solid Oxide Fuel Cell) technology is highly suitable for power plant
purposes.[Rok15c] It is of interest with regards to a fuel cell for power plant
purposes, that the efficiency is high. The solid oxide fuel cell is significant in
this case, yielding efficiencies of 60% and above, due to its high range working
temperatures up to 1000◦C depending on the type of electrolyte used.[Rok12]
The electrolyte used in the SOFC is a solid ceramic material, which works as a
membrane allowing the passage of oxygen ions. In this case the electrolyte has
named the SOFC.
Figure 2.15 shows the basic principle of the fuel cell. Oxygen is fed to the cath-
ode, and negative oxygen ions can move through the electrolyte and allow for a
chemical conversion of the fuel in the anode. The free electrons from the conver-
sion of oxidant and fuel are led to an electrical circuit, hence power production
is available. [Rok15c]
Furthermore, figure 2.15 includes some of the fuels that can be utilized in an
SOFC. The following equations 2.7,2.8,2.9 and 2.10 break down the chemical
reactions of the fuel in the SOFC.
For hydrogen, equation 2.7
2H2 + 2O−−⇒2H2O + 2e−
(2.7)
For carbon monoxide, equation 2.8
2CO + 2O−−⇒2CO2 + 2e−
(2.8)
And for methane reforming, equation 2.9 and 2.10
CH4 + H2O ⇒3H2 + CO
(2.9)
==PAGE:44==
26
2 Technology Overview
Figure 2.15: The principle of the SOFC technology [Rok15c]
or
CH4 + 2H2O ⇒4H2 + CO2
(2.10)
depending on the water partial pressure. As one may notice, in equation 2.9,
the methane is broken down, and the reactant is hydrogen and carbon monoxide,
which then reacts as in equation 2.7 and 2.8. If methane reforms as in equation
2.10, only hydrogen is further converted as in equation 2.7. [Ste12]
The use of fossil fuels in an SOFC will contribute to CO2 emissions as seen in the
chemical formulas above. However, the relative lower than conventional power
producing technologies based on Rankine and Brayton cycles, which is explained
by the SOFCs high electric efficiency. [Ste12]
The solid oxide fuel cell has large advantages due to its flexibility using different
fuels, however heavy carbons must be broken down. This can be done in the
SOFC by internal reforming, however to limit volatility, with regards to thermal
shock in the SOFC as a result of endothermic reactions, preprocessing the fuel is
an option. This processing, also called reforming, can be done in different ways.
Catalytic Partial Oxidation (CPO) is a technology that uses compressed air. The
reformation process is exothermic, hence increasing the temperature of the fuel
reformed. Another technology used is the adiabatic steam reformer (ASR). The
ASR uses preheated steam and preforms an endothermic reaction when reforming.
[Rok13]
The cells in an SOFC are put together in stacks due to the low voltage of single
==PAGE:45==
2.6 Summary
27
cells. One cell has a cell voltage of maximum 1V. Fuel cell stacks are a solution to
the low voltage, where many cells are coupled in series letting the voltage grow for
each cell. Tubular and planar are the two dominant SOFC designs. The planar
design is in a rectangular shape stacked on top of each other, which gives smaller
losses than the tubular design. In this project the planar SOFC technology is
assumed sufficient.
Other benefits of the SOFC technology are that it does not contain moving parts.
Therefore it does not creating noise or vibration and is therefore not prone to
stress with non- mechanical parts. This ensures high endurance and reliability
leading to less maintenance. [Rok14]
The SOFC technology has been studied in different hybrid systems with steam
turbines, gas turbines, internal as well as external combustion engines, solar power
systems and heat pumps. [Rok15c] [Rok15b] In this analysis it will be investigated
with a steam cycle.
2.6
Summary
The technologies that will be used further in this project have been explained
briefly. The steam cycle is the original plant that will be repowered. As men-
tioned, a slightly modified version of the Asnæs Power Plant block 2 will be
modelled.
As mentioned, integrating desalination in a steam cycle is considered as an area
of investigation, hereby creating a dual-purpose power plant. A direct contact
membrane distillation system will be integrated with the steam cycle, as the
preferred desalination technology, where ever it is possible to recover heat. The
different options for recovering heat for potable water production will be analysed
and discussed further.
The conventional gas turbine technology is introduced and different optimizations
and configurations are presented briefly. The simple gas turbine has been applied
in combined cycle plants and as a topping cycle in repowering cases, which will
be investigated further.
Fuel cell technology and specifically the SOFC configuration is introduced and
explained.
It is a relatively new technology with regards to being used as a
topping cycle for repowering purposes.
The technologies mentioned above will be implemented in the case study, mod-
elled and analysed in the latter.
==PAGE:46==
28
==PAGE:47==
CHAPTER 3
Methods and
Modelling
The following chapter will describe the methodology and modelling for the base
case system setup, consisting of a steam cycle power plant integrated with a
desalination system, along with the repowering solutions as mentioned; the gas
turbine cycle, the SOFC plant and the PTSC with biomass gasification plant.
3.1
Dynamic Network Analysis
The thermodynamic simulation tool DNA - Dynamic Network Analysis - is used
to model and simulate the mentioned energy systems. DNA was developed as a
research project at DTU. [EH05] The DNA software is an open platform software
written in FORTRAN77, giving the user the possibility to broaden the programs
capabilities by introducing new components. Hence the software is continously
under development to ensure future applicability.
DNA is a component based thermodynamic simulation tool, meaning the necces-
sary component is input into a model connecting components with nodes, which
requires certain parameters and additional conditions (ADDCO) in order to run.
A library of components is incorporated in the programme and consists of e.g.
turbo machinery, heatexchangers, gasifiers, condensers, burners, fuel cells, en-
gines, valves and more specialized components and utility components. Apart
from components, the software includes different medias, such as combustibles
or working fluids all phases - solids, liquids and gases.
Furthermore, besides
the components, additional boundary conditions and start guesses related to the
components are neccessary for the simulation to run and converge.
In order for the software to simulate the thermodynamical model, a set of math-
ematical equations are written into the programming of the components based
on thermodynamic theory and these are numerially solved. These equations are
==PAGE:48==
30
3 Methods and Modelling
based on the laws of thermodynamics, such as the conservation of mass and
energy. [Elm99]
3.2
Base Case
The base case is in this project a cogeneration (dual purpose) power plant gen-
erating power from a steam cycle and using the produced heat for desalinating
sea water. The configuration of the steam cycle and desalination plants are seen
below, as well as the modelling.
3.2.1
Steam Cycle Configuration
The steam cycle plant or base case is a slightly modified version of the Asnæs
Power Plant block 2 (see Appendix A).
The steam cycle is fuelled with coal in a furnace. The boiler heats, boils and
super heats the pressurized water through three heat exchangers; an economizer,
an evaporator and a super heater. The steam loop consists of two high pres-
sure turbines, an intermediate pressure turbine and three low pressure turbines.
Besides this, it has a condenser to condense the steam back to saturated water
before it enters into a deaerator, which is an open feedwater heater, where in-
filtrated gases are removed from the feedwater, by feeding in steam at a higher
temperature and pressure than the feedwater. The feedwater passes through a
pump to pressurize the water. The steam cycle also has the ability to deliver
process heat. This steam is extracted after the IP turbine and heats a loop of
water through a heat exchanger that is delivered to costumers.
There are three potential locations for extracting heat to integrate the desalina-
tion system. These are listed below and will be examined in the latter.
• flue gas from the furnace
• process heat
• condenser
The steam cycle is as mentioned a closed loop. Following the steam, it leaves
the boiler at a pressure level of 145 bar and 488◦C at node 1 before entering
a splitter, splitting the superheated steam equally (node 2 & 4) into two high
pressure turbines (turbine 101 and 102). The steam is then expanded through
the HP turbines to a pressure level of 10 bar (node 3 & 5). After the HP expansion
==PAGE:49==
3.2 Base Case
31
the steam from each HP turbine is combined in a mixer and joined in node 6 and
then split again, with the purpose of sending 10% of the steam to the deaerator
(node 7) and the remaining 90% to the intermediate pressure turbine (node 8).
The steam is expanded through the IP turbine (103) to a pressure level of 1.8
bar (node 9).
Then it is again split where a fraction (node 10), initially 9%
of the steam mass flow, is used as process heat - heat exchanged through a
heat exchanger (306) at approximately 100◦C (node 43). The remaining (node
11) continues to the low pressure turbine, where it is expanded at either back
pressure mode to 0.6 bar and condensing mode to 0.06 bar. The condensed water
then proceeds to the deaerator where the gases are removed and temperature
is raised to 97.72◦C in condensing mode and 140.87◦C in back-pressure mode
before entering the feedwater pump, lifting the water pressure to 165 bar before
entering the economizer (node 15), the evaporator (node 16) and the superheater
(node 17).
Outside the steam loop, the boiler or steam generator is heated by the combus-
tion of coal in a furnace. Air enters a compressor at 20◦C (component 313) and
is preheated in the air preheater (component 312) by combustion flue-gases be-
fore entering the furnace. Coal enters the furnace (node 28) at 25◦C and the
combustion creates flue-gas and ash (node 29). The flue-gas supplies heat to the
superheater (node 23), the evaporator (node 24) and the economizer (node 25).
Furthermore the remaining heat, preheats the air for combustion, however the
flue gas has a lower limit at the stack temperature of Tstack = 120.2◦C.
The stack temperature should not be below Tstack = 120.2◦C in order to avoid
formation of corrosive sulphuric acid from the mixture of sulphur oxide and steam.
[Wik09] [Rok10] And hence it is set as a constraint.
3.2.2
Steam Cycle Modelling
The steam cycle plant integrated with a desalination system for heat recovery is
described and modelled in DNA. Boundary conditions and parameters significant
to the components comprising of the model are presented.
Certain values, inputs as well as boundary conditions, are necessary for the model
to resemble the actual plant and they are given in table 3.1.
The values seen in table 3.1 are input in DNA and the plant is modelled to target
a power output of 142MW. The high temperature steam from the super heater
T1, the live steam pressure entering the HP turbine p1, the live steam pressure
from the LP turbine p12 and the flue gas temperature exiting the boiler Tstack
are set as ADDCOs in DNA. By increasing the mass flow of the steam at node
1, ˙m1,steam, the wanted power output of 142MWe can be acheived.

The DCMD system is seen in the BoP in figure 3.2. Following the seawater, it

(node 53), the condenser (305) functions as the primary seawater heater.

side. Permeate is then circulated out of the DCMD, reduced in temperature in
the seawater preheater (351) to 25◦C creating a loop of cooled permeate. The
temperature difference being the driving force of the DCMD unit.

3.3 Gas Turbine Topping Cycle
39
3.3
Gas Turbine Topping Cycle
In order to study a simple gas turbine topping cycle solution applicable with the
bottoming cycle base case, three different commercial simple gas turbines are
found and solutions for all three, including supplementary firing if necessary, are
studied.
3.3.1
Gas Turbine Configuration
A simple gas turbine is chosen, as mentioned earlier, for repowering the bottoming
cycle. The gas turbine will replace the coal furnace and the exhaust gases from the
GT are utilized to heat the feed water to the saturation point in the economizer,
evaporate the water to saturated steam in the evaporator and finally superheat
the saturated steam, in the superheater. The superheated high pressure steam
then resumes the same cycle, as for the case with the bottoming cycle, to the
HP, IP and LP turbines producing power and desalinating sea water. In terms
of repowering, the superheater, evaporator and economizer will be referred to as
the Heat Recovery Steam Generator (HRSG). Furthermore, depending on the
amount of gas turbines that are input for the given case, a supplementary firing
burner can be input and add energy to the exhaust gases, if more is necessary to
drive the bottoming cycle. The choice of adding an extra gas turbine or letting a
supplementary firing burner supply the remaining energy comes down to a trade
off between a higher cost of adding a GT and accepting a lower efficiency with
supplementary firing. [Rok15e]
A BoP of the gas turbine topping cycle is seen in figure 3.4. Depending on the
setup, one GT may not suffice.
Turbine
173
Compressor
171
70
71
Burner 372
73
74
72
Fuel inlet
Figure 3.4: A BoP of the simple gas turbine topping cycle
==PAGE:58==
40
3 Methods and Modelling
Following the air, it enters the GT (node 70) and is compressed in the compressor
(171) according to the given pressure ratio from the chosen GT. The compressed
air (node 71) enters the combustion chamber or burner (component 372), where
natural gas is added (node 72) and combustion occurs. The combustion creates
an off-gas at a certain temperature, which will be modelled and displayed in the
results for each GT. The off-gas then expands over the turbine (373) creating
power. If more than one gas turbine is implemented, then the exhaust gas from
all gas turbines are connected in a mixer, before entering the HRSG. And as
mentioned, if it is deemed necessary to integrate a supplementary firing burner,
the mixed exhaust gas (node 75) will enter the supplementary firing burner (374)
lifting the temperature to the necessary level in order to drive the bottoming
cycle. The flue gas (23) is then fed to the HRSG (node 23-26) with a sufficient
off-gas temperature.
The flue gas exiting the HRSG (node 26) is expected to be higher than the stack
temperature, but it will vary based on the type and amount of GTs chosen for
the repowering solution and to what extent the supplementary firing will play a
role. Furthermore the flue gas can be used for the flue gas DCMD connection,
which will be studied. A BoP of a GT repowering solution with 2 GTs, connected
and integrated with supplementary firing before fuelling the bottoming cycle is
seen in figure 3.5.
3.3.2
Gas Turbine Modelling
In order to model the gas turbines, they are input in DNA and the parameters
are adjusted to fit the exact chosen GT with regards to power output, exhaust
gas temperature and efficiency. The preliminary values used for modelling the
GTs are seen in table 3.8. These parameters are just used as start values and they
are assumed decent start values based on table values by Wiksten (2009). They
will be modified along the way and the resulting fitted values for parameters will
be seen in the results section 4.2 for each chosen GT. [Wik09]
With the energy found, required to maintain driving the bottoming cycle, differ-

Furthermore, a temperature difference of approximately ∆Tpinch = 10◦C is input

cold stream and the hot stream is most constrained. In some cases it was neces-
sary to place it at the economizer, because the minimum temperature difference
between the exhaust gas and the steam was at the economizer and not evapo-
rator. This is regulated by the temperature in the supplementary firing burner.

NG exporting countries, remove substances such as sulphur from the NG, prior
to export, due to the problematic features suplhur has on materials with regards
to corrosion. However, the composition of the natural gas as seen in table 3.9 is
assumed sufficient and is without sulphur. [Rok15d]

3 Methods and Modelling
3.4
SOFC Topping Cycle
An SOFC topping cycle will be sized and studied as a repowering solution. The
configuration of the SOFC as a topping cycle will be explained in the latter as
well as the modelling in order to perform simulations on the given setup.
3.4.1
SOFC Configuration
The topping cycle used to repower the base case power plant is a fuel cell of
the solid oxide type as mentioned earlier run on natural gas. The topping cycle
consists of an SOFC as well as components that reform and preheat the fuel and
air prior to entering the anode and cathode. An overview of the topping cycle
can be seen in the PI diagram in figure 3.6.
SOFC 382
Cathode
Anode
CPO
381
RP
380
CP
387
Burner 383
Fuel Inlet
Comp
384
80
81
83
84
82
85
86
23
93
94
92
90
91
Air Inlet
Comp
385
89
87
88
Air Inlet
83
Figure 3.6: A BoP of the solid oxide fuel cell topping cycle
The topping cycle is driven by the SOFC fuelled by natural gas to the anode
side and oxygen to the cathode, respectively. The fuel is released into the cycle
(node 80) at 15◦C and 8 bar and is first preheated in a reformer preheater (RP)
which is a heat exchanger that captures heat from the off-fuel from the anode.
==PAGE:63==
3.4 SOFC Topping Cycle
45
Preheating is necessary, due to the high operating temperature of the SOFC, and
thereby avoid mechanical stress with large temperature changes in the stacks.
[Rok13] The preheated fuel (node 81) then enters the reformer, Catalytic Partial
Oxidation reformer (CPO). Depending on the type of reformer, it requires a
certain temperature in order to properly reform the fuel. In this case the fuel
should contain a temperature of 525◦C before entering the CPO, as seen in table
3.10.
The fuel then enters the CPO reformer (381) which is a catalyst that breaks
down heavy hydrocarbons such as C2H6, C3H8 and C4H10. In this system a
Catalytic Partial Oxidation reformer is used. The reformation in the CPO is a
chemical reaction of the exothermic type, meaning it releases heat in the process of
reforming. Due to the exothermic reactions, the temperature will rise from 525◦C
to the anode inlet temperature, 650◦C, as seen in table 3.10. The limitation with
regards to temperature around the CPO reformer will be commented on in the
analysis, when attempting to optimize the operating temperature of the fuel
cell. What characterizes a CPO is that it uses compressed air in the reformation
process comparing it to that of an Adiabatic Steam Reformer (ASR) which uses
steam. Air enters (node 85) a compressor, which continues into the CPO (node
86) to assist the process of reforming. The heavy hydrocarbons are then broken
down and the fuel consists mainly of the combustibles CH4, H2 and CO and is
now ready to be fed to the anode of the fuel cell (node 82). [Rok15c]
The fuel proceeds through the fuel cell and due to exothermic reactions between
anode, cathode, fuel and oxidant the operating temperature is 780◦C, as seen in
table 3.10. After fueling the SOFC, there still will remain a certain amount of
fuel in the off gas (node 83) depending on the utilization factor, which is why the
term off-fuel is used of the product exiting the anode. The utilization factor will
be studied further in the analysis, but for inital calculations it will be set to 0.8,
see table 3.10. The off-fuel will have the temperature equivalent of the operating
temperature and therefore it will be used as the hot source in the RP to preheat
the fuel. The off-fuel will then proceed to the burner (node 84) - if there were no
bottoming cycle, the fuel would still have to be burned out due to restrictions on
fuel content in fluegas. [Rok15c]
The fuel used in the SOFC is natural gas identical to the fuel used in the GT
topping cycle solution. And the same chemical composition for natural gas can
be referred to, as in table 3.9. It is important to mention that the natural gas,
as defined in DNA, doesn’t include sulphur. Certain producers of natural gas,
remove sulphur on site prior to supplying it. However, if sulphur is present in
the natural gas used, a desulphurizer should be included in the SOFC cycle. A
desulphurizer is a catalyst which has the function of removing the sulphur content
in natural gas and preventing SOFC poisoning. [Rok12]
==PAGE:64==
46
3 Methods and Modelling
Looking at the SOFC from the cathode side, fresh air at ambient temperature of
20◦C and 1.013 bar (node 87) enters a compressor to drive the air through the
system. Two setups of the SOFC topping cycle are presented and will be studied
initially to evaluate which of the two perform better. The first setup, as seen in
figure 3.6, blows fresh air through the cathode compressor (385) and directly into
the cathode preheater (CP) raising the temperature to 600◦C (node 90), which
is the cathode inlet temperature, see table 3.10. The cathode ’off air’, air leaving
the SOFC, has a temperature of 780◦C due to exothermic reactions as mentioned
previously (node 91). This off air is used to preheat the fresh air in the CP and
then continues through a splitter where a fraction enters the burner (node 93)
for combustion of the off fuel. The splitter has a vital function of controlling the
fluegas temperature in the burner - if a high flue gas temperature is demanded
from the burner after combustion, then the splitter leads less off air to the burner,
and opposite if relatively low flue gas temperature is demanded.

3 Methods and Modelling
Calculating the SOFC cells voltage efficiency is done by defining the SOFC system
performance as seen below. The power production depends on the magnitude of
fuel fed to the anode and it is seen in equation 3.13, where Uf is the utilization
factor of the fuel cell, ηv is the voltage efficiency and ηrev is the reversible efficiency.
[Rok14]
PSOF C = ηrev·ηv·Uf
(
LHVH2· ˙ηH2,in+LHVCO· ˙ηCOin+LHVCH4· ˙ηCH4,in
)
(3.13)
The reversible or ideal efficiency of the fuel cell is seen in equation 3.14, which is
seen as the change in the Gibbs free energy divided by the lower heating value of
the fuel - the maximum obtainable efficiency.
ηrev = ∆Gfuel
LHVfuel
(3.14)
The electric efficiency of the plant is then calculated as seen in equation 3.15,
defined as the net power produced (the power produced subtracted by the power
consumed for auxiliary components) divided by the product of the mass flow and
lower heating value of the fuel.
ηelec,SOF C = PSOF C −Paux
˙mNG · LHVNG
(3.15)
As mentioned, two setups of the SOFC topping cycle are presented, seen in figure
3.6 and 3.7, and will be studied initially to evaluate which of the two perform
better. For further optimization only the better performing of the two SOFC
topping cycle setups will be analysed. The SOFC including a hybrid recuperator
is expected to perform better and will therefore be further optimized.
The SOFC including the hybrid recuperator will be analysed by optimizing it on
certain parameters, including the utilization factor, the operating temperature
and the amount of stacks.

the Asnæs Power Plant block 2 as mentioned. Then it is considered with regards
to heat recovery, where this is possible and to which extent.
When the extent of the heat recovery is clarified the DCMD system is integrated

CHAPTER 4
Results
The following chapter displays and discusses the results and study of the base
case system setup, consisting of a steam cycle power plant integrated with a
desalination system, along with the repowering solutions as mentioned; the gas
turbine cycle and the SOFC plant.
4.1
Base Case
The results for the base case are first calculated for the steam cycle alone prior to
implementing the DCMD system. The potential for recovering heat is presented
and the DCMD is then implemented in different configurations - at the condenser,
process heater and flue-gas. The DCMD configurations are studied and evaluated
before repowering solutions are considered.
4.1.1
Steam Cycle
Initially the steam cycle is modelled and simulated without the DCMD system
integrated. The steam cycle is sized based on the input values mentioned in the
methods in table 3.1.
• steam from super heater, at temperature T1 = 488◦C
• the live steam pressure entering the HP turbine p1 = 145 bar
• the live steam pressure from the LP turbine p12 = 0.06 bar
• the flue gas temperature exiting the boiler Tstack = 120.2◦C
The plant is modelled to target a power output of 142 MW net power in con-
densing mode, meaning producing power alone. For the steam cycle to produce
the target 142 MW, the mass flow of the steam is found at ˙m1,steam = 132.5kg/s.
==PAGE:70==
52
4 Results
The results obtained from the initial steam cycle simulations, prior to integrat-
ing the DCMD system, can be seen in the pie charts in figure 4.1 showing power
production, heat production and the energy not recovered.
(a) Condensing mode producing 142.27 MW
power without heat production
(b) Condensing mode producing 137.58 MW
power and 25.72 MJ/s process heat
(c) Backpressure mode producing 110.86 MW, 25.72 MJ/s process heat and 211.07 MJ/s re-
coverable heat
Figure 4.1: The energy production of the steam cycle in different settings, prior
to integrating the DCMD system
The results seen in the pie charts in figure 4.1 show three different simulations
of the steam cycle prior to DCMD integration. Initially the steam cycle is sized
based on the condensing mode without heat prodution, and it is modelled to pro-
duce a net power output of 142.27 MW as mentioned, which is seen in figure 4.1a
with a mass flow of ˙m1,steam = 132.5kg/s as mentioned previously. Furthermore,
the electric efficiency reaches 35.74% as seen in table 4.1. Table 4.1 displays an
==PAGE:71==
4.1 Base Case
53
overview of power production, heat production, electric efficiency and CHP effi-
ciency for all three settings. The mass flow is essential for the sizing of the plant,
hence it will remain constant throughout all simulations. By using a steam mass
flow of 132.5kg/s throughout simulations in this project, the steam cycle settings
are now set, and the cycle will essentially benefit from the same energy input
into the cycle. This energy can however be managed differently or appropriately
to fit a certain demand, which is seen in the following pie charts.
In figure 4.1b the steam cycle is set to produce process heat in condensing mode,
which is a high temperature heat recovered at approximately T43 = 100 −110◦C.
The process heat is provided by diverting 9% of the steam mass flow to a heat
exchanger after the IP turbine at p10 = 1.8 bar and T10 = 116.9◦C. This produces
25.72 MJ/s heat, but at the ”cost” of less net power produced, namely 137.58
MW. Hence, 4.69 MW is ”paid” for 25.72 MJ/s process heat. The steam cycle in
condensing mode with process heat production has a electric efficiency of 34.74%
and an energy efficiency of 41.23% as seen in table 4.1.
Finally, the plant is set at back-pressure mode, still producing the same amount
of process heat as the previous setting, and the expansion process is now stopped
at p12 = 0.6 bar in the LP turbine with the potential of recovering more thermal
energy, as seen in figure 4.1c. It should be mentioned that in the back-pressure
mode, at 0.6 bar, the temperature at T12 is 85.95◦C and therefore it can be recov-
ered, and in this project will be used for desalination purposes. In conventional
CHP plants, the most common purpose for the heat is district heating or process
heat for industrial purposes, but even district cooling using absorption chillers
is receiving more attention in contemporary all-purpose plants. [GP15] So in
back-pressure mode, the plant produces a mere net power of 110.86 MW, with
25.72 MJ/s process heat, however the heat transferred in the condenser can now
be recovered due to the usable high temperatures, and potentially up to 211.07
MJ/s can be recovered as seen in figure 4.1c. When taking the condenser heat
production into account, the potential recoverable heat amounts to a sum of the
process heat and recoverable heat from the condenser - an aggregate potential
heat production of 236.79 MJ/s as seen in table 4.1. The electric efficiency drops
to a mere 29.76%, because less enthalpy is converted to mechanical energy in
the LP turbine when stopping the expansion process earlier - at 0.6 bar.The en-
ergy efficiency, however, takes the aggregate heat production into account and
reaches a high of 93.33%, and as seen in table 4.1 it is much higher than the plant
efficiency in the other settings.
It is important to evaluate if this drop in net power production is accepted in
the backpressure mode as a ”cost” for the potential recoverable heat produced
in return. This is considered acceptable, because in repowering power cycles, the
goal is to produce more overall power and reduce the carbon dioxide emissions,
==PAGE:72==
54
4 Results
CO2/kW h, preferably at a higher electric efficiency.
And this will also be the
case when repowering with another power producing topping cycle, because the
topping cycle will contribute to the surplus power production.[ZDB12]

In the condensing modes, the turbine expands the steam to 0.06 bar and the
temperature is reduced to approximately 36.2◦C and therefore it is considered
too low to be recovered in this project.
Throughout the past decade, research and implementation, to some extent, has
been made on recovering waste heat or using district heating at low temperatures.
[Lun+10] This gives the option of recovering and using low temperatures, eg. by
expanding steam to pressures closer to those in condensing mode, approximately
around 0.1 bar, and still being able to recover energy from the condenser. Energy
could also be used from other sources, such as recovery from data centres (server
rooms or other cooling rooms).[Wah+17] Temperatures around 50◦C have been
tested and implemented in district heating networks.[Ols+14] These are catego-
rized as 4th generation district heating.
[Sch+17] Even temperatures around
40 −45◦C are being discussed as an option for low temperature district heating,
however implications are found with bacterial growth when maintaining potable
water at these temperatures in hot water storage tanks. [Ols+14] The 3rd gener-
ation district heating networks are, however, still dominating in the majority of
==PAGE:73==
4.1 Base Case
55
district heating grids today, operating in temperature intervals of approximately
75 −90◦C.[Wah+17]
The steam cycle in back-pressure mode, producing 110.86 MW and recoverable
heat potentially adding up to 236.79 MJ/s is chosen as the only setting for further
simulations, because this option had the highest energy efficiency as expected.
The drop in electric efficiency and loss in net power output is accepted in the
bottoming cycle, because when repowered, the power output, and to some extent
the electric efficiency, will be boosted by the topping cycle.
4.1.2
DCMD integration in steam cycle
As mentioned in the methods and seen in figure 4.1 and table 4.1, there are
several places that heat can be recovered for desalination purposes. These points
are as mentioned
• the flue gas from the burner - node 27
• the process heat - componenet 305, node 53
• condenser heat - component 306, node 43
The DCMD system is integrated in the steam cycle at all three points initially:
the flue gas at T27 = 160◦C after the air preheater, the process heat exchanger
(9% of the steam flow) and the condenser which from now on will be in backpres-
sure mode as mentioned earlier, p12 = 0.6 bar. The performance of the initial
calculation of the steam cycle with the DCMD system is seen in table 4.2.
