4 Results
As seen in table 4.2, the net power production, process heat and condenser heat
production are all affected slightly by the integration of a DCMD system at all
three mentioned points. This can be seen when comparing the results to those of
the back-pressure mode, in table 4.1, of the steam cycle without DCMD included.
The loss in net power output is caused by power consumption in circulation
pumps in the DCMD units. Furthermore, in the results for the back-pressure
mode without DCMD included, the steam cycle used flue gas heat in the air
preheater (component 312) and recovered heat from it until stack temperature
Tstack = 120.2◦C. Therefore less fuel was needed for the same energy to be
converted in the steam cycle due to recycling heat by air preheating with it. So
in the case where a DCMD unit is coupled to the flue gas after the air preheater
at T27 = 160◦C, keeping in mind that the stack temperature remains at Tstack =
120.2◦C, the heat used for the flue gas DCMD is not recycled heat, but heat at
a price of more coal expended in the coal burner, which explains the slight drop
in electric efficiency to 29.08%.
The sum of the water production leads to 76.33 kg/s.
Assuming water has a
density of 1000 kg/m3 and a litre is equal to a dm3, then the conversion is

A total of approximately 6.6 million litres/day is considered a high production
of potable water, however mega plants have been commissioned and are in use
in the Middle East producing much larger scales of power and potable water.
For instance, the ’Ras Al-Khair Power and Desalination Plant’ located in Saudi
Arabia, boasts a net power capacity of 2400 MW and potable water production
capacity of 728 million litres/day. [Wat14] Another dual-purpose mega plant built
is the ’Fujairah 1 Independant Water and Power Plant’ located in the United Arab
Emirates which has a capacity of 882 MW net power output and approximately
591 million litres/day. Both these plants apply a hybrid desalination technology,
by integrating reverse osmosis and multi-stage flash, hence obtaining an optimum
potable water production for their given cases. [Wat15]
Out of the three points of heat extraction for the DCMD system, which will be
investigated and discussed, an evaluation will determine which are considered fea-
sible and which are redundant. First the flue gas DCMD connection is evaluated
by exchanging heat from the flue gas to a DCMD system at temperatures varied
from 160 −130◦C - a diagram of the connection can be seen in figure 4.2. The
effect of including a flue gas DCMD system is seen in figures 4.3a, 4.3b and 4.3c.
As seen in figure 4.3a, the electric efficiency of the steam cycle increases slightly
from 29.08% to 29.55% as the flue-gas temperature entering the DCMD is de-
creased from 160 −130◦C. Simultaneously, a decrease in the fuel consumption

Figure 4.2: A BoP of the DCMD system recovering energy from the flue gas
is observed, spanning from 376.45 MJ/s at 160◦C to 370.96 MJ/s 130◦C. As
explained previously, this is due to the fact that the heat used for the flue-gas
DCMD is not recycled heat, but heat that comes at a price of more coal expended
in the coal burner, so when less energy is recovered in the flue-gas DCMD sys-
tem, the fuel consumption drops as well. Which is linked closely to the increase
in electric efficiency, because less fuel is used to reach the same work output in
the steam cycle.
The total power consumption as well as the power consumed for the air compres-
sor (component 313) can be seen in figure 4.3b. When the flue-gas temperature
is reduced, the total power consumption decreases from 10.17 MW to 10.05 MW.
The same trend is observed for the air compressor power consumption, which
drops from 7.59 MW to 7.48 MW. When the flue-gas temperature entering the
flue-gas DCMD is high, 160◦C, less energy is recovered in the air preheater. In
order to maintain the same enthalpy difference over the superheater, evaporator
and economizer, the mass flow of air is increased, which has an immediate effect
on the air compressor power consumed. And that same consumption obviously
affects the total power consumption.

function of changing the flue gas temperature entering the DCMD. When the
temperature is decreased from 160 −130◦C the water production drops from
2.19 kg/s to 0.53 kg/s. It should be mentioned here, that the amount of potable
water produced from the process heat exchanger and condenser is 7.46 kg/s and
66.69 kg/s respectively. The production of potable water from the process heat
exchanger and condenser is unchanged when changing the flue-gas temperature

==PAGE:77==
4.1 Base Case
59
overall production of potable water, apart from the fact that it reduces electric
efficiency, and therefore it is eliminated from the design in the base case. Keeping
in mind that a techno-economic analysis isn’t included in this project, the net
gain of producing potable water from the flue-gas, comes at the cost of an extra
heat exchanger and installation. This, however, is not the case with the process
heater and condenser, these components are already existing in the original steam
cycle design and do not contribute to extra investment costs.
The flue-gas DCMD is removed from the base case and now the steam cycle
with DCMD system recovering heat from the process heater and the condenser
is evaluated.
The amount of mass flow that is directed to the process heater is set in the
component during simulations. This value is now varied up to 45 % of the total
steam flow, 132.5 kg/s.
Figure 4.4 shows the potable water production from
the process heater, condenser and the sum of both, when the fraction of steam
mass flow directed to the process heater is increased.
As expected the water
produced from the process heat exchanger grows when the fraction of steam flow
is increased to it. And likewise, steam flow is diverted away from the LP turbine
and the condenser, hence the water production from the condenser heat drops
as the mass flow of the steam is increased to the process heater. The sum of the
produced water, is however maintained constant, as figure 4.4 also shows. And
therefore, increasing or varying mass flow to the process heater has no affect on
the potable water production.
Figure 4.4: Potable water production from the process heat and condenser heat
as a function of the fraction of steam led to the process heater
Varying mass flow to the process heater does however have a slight affect on the
electric efficiency and net power production of the steam cycle. This is seen in
==PAGE:78==
60
4 Results
figure 4.5.
As steam mass flow is increased to the process heater, less steam
reaches the LP turbines and the result of this is a drop in net power production
from approximately 112 MW to 104 MW. The electric efficiency also sees a fall,
from 30.2% to 28.6%, and hence keeping the fraction of steam mass flow at 9%
to the process heater (as it initially was set to) is now accepted as a constant
design setting throughout the next simulations and repowering cases.
Figure 4.5: Net power production and electric efficiency as a function of increasing
steam mass flow to process heater
The last point where heat can be recovered for desalination purposes is at the
condenser.
Running the LP turbine at back-pressure mode has been chosen
as a design setting earlier and the argument for using the recovered heat from
the condenser is undeniable. The choice of running at back-pressure was made
based on the increase in efficiency, and this efficiency is only gained if the heat
is recovered from the condenser. Therefore the DCMD system will recover heat
from the condenser and this doesn’t need to be investigated further.
The final setting is chosen with a DCMD system connected to the process heat
exchanger, diverting and recovering heat from 9% of the steam mass flow, and
connected to the condenser as seen in the methods in figure 3.3. The performance
results for the final design of the bottoming cycle are seen in table 4.3.
Prior to repowering the bottoming cycle, the base case now remains the same
with the chosen configuration for net power output and water output as seen in
table 4.3.
The DCMD flue-gas unit has been removed from the base case prior to repowering.
However, when repowering cases are introduced - depending on the topping cycle
chosen - there may be a potential for recovering some heat from the mass flow
and temperature of the topping cycle’s working media that is available out of the

The coal furnace is removed from the bottoming cycle and instead of a boiler
driven by coal off-gases, the different topping cycles are integrated, by letting
their exhaust gases flow through the economizer, evaporator and super heater. In
repowering cases it is referred to as a Heat Recovery Steam Generator (HRSG)
as mentioned earlier.
The heat needed to drive the bottoming cycle can be
found calculating the energy required over the economizer, evaporator and the
superheater. Using equation 3.3 this is calculated to be approximately 352 MJ/s,
and it is considered the minimum energy required to drive the steam cycle. This
energy is simply a guideline when designing a topping cycle and the energy used
to drive the bottoming cycle is expected to exceed this amount. Furthermore,
with regards to coal burners, it should be mentioned that coal is combusted at
extremely high temperatures and the furnaces are designed to manage these high
temperatures - in this case T23,coal = 1973.16◦C. In repowering cases with gas
turbines or solid oxide fuel cells, the exhaust gases have much lower temperatures.
The temperature demanded of the steam loop is set to T1,steam = 488◦C (see Ap-
pendix A). The temperature of the off-gas, T23, entering the superheater, should
be higher than this. A terminal temperature difference of ∆Tterminal = 25◦C is
assumed sufficient. With respect to the terminal temperature difference, the top-
ping cycle off-heat temperature should be at least T23,minimum = 488+25 = 513◦,
which is set as a boundary condition for designing an appropriate topping cycle
solution. [Rok15a]

62
4 Results
4.2
Gas Turbine Repowering
Three different simple gas turbines are selected from the gas turbine specification
catalogue produced by ’Gas Turbine World’ [GTW13]. They are chosen based on
their power output and exhaust temperature, because these factors are related
to the amount of energy available in the exhaust gas for driving the bottom-
ing cycle. The gas turbines selected for repowering purposes are the Mitsubishi
M501DA, the GE Energy MS7001EA and the Alstom GT13E2. They all vary in
performance and specifications, such as power output, mass flow of working fluid,
exhaust temperature and electric efficiency. These specifications are essential to
how the gas turbines will perform when integrated as repowering solutions with
the bottoming cycle. The gas turbines are now modelled targeting the catalogue
specifications and performances.
4.2.1
Choosing Gas Turbines and Initial Modelling Results
The first GT modelled is the Mitsubishi M501DA and the catalogue specifications
as well as the modelled specifications calculated are seen in table 4.4. The mass
flow through the turbine as well as the pressure ratio over the compressor are
used as input values in DNA. The electric efficiency, power output and exhaust
temperature are modelled results. Furthermore, the error between the catalogue
value and the real value is displayed in the column farthest to the right. The errors
are extremely small, only the power output differs by a notable 1.44 %, however
the modelled value is lower than the catalogue value, hence the conservative
approach is accepted.

4.2.2
Different Gas Turbine Repowering Solutions
The choice of adding an extra gas turbine or letting a supplementary firing burner
supply the remaining energy comes down to a trade off between a higher cost of
adding a GT and accepting a lower efficiency with supplementary firing.
In
essence it is a question of feasibility - from an engineering perspective techno-
economics and thermodynamics should both be fields considered when investing
and commissioning power plants, however a techno-economic assessment isn’t
realised in this project.
The strategy with regards to repowering the bottoming cycle was to create dif-
ferent cases with each gas turbine model. The Mitsubishi M501DA, GE Energy
MS7001EA and Alstom GT13E2 all have different exhaust temperatures and
mass flows as seen in tables 4.4, 4.5 and 4.6. Therefore they will have different
setups, depending on how many GTs will suffice driving the bottoming cycle,
and in which cases it is necessary with supplementary firing. Seen below are the
setups simulated for each chosen gas turbine.
Mitsubishi M501DA
• 1 GT incl. large contribution of supplementary firing
• 2 GTs incl. small contribution of supplementary firing
• 3 GTs no supplementary firing
GE Energy MS7001EA
• 1 GT incl. large contribution of supplementary firing
==PAGE:83==
4.2 Gas Turbine Repowering
65
• 2 GTs incl. small contribution of supplementary firing
• 3 GTs no supplementary firing
Alstom GT13E2
• 1 GT incl. small contribution of supplementary firing
• 2 GT no supplementary firing
The repowering cases are rigid and cannot be ’optimized’ in the HRSG. The GT
parameters are set, and therefore the mass flow and the exhaust temperature
should be able to drive the bottoming cycle, but the HRSG will not recover
energy from the exhaust gas until it reaches the stack temperature. This is why,
repowering in essence doesn’t boast as high electric efficiency as pre-designed
combined cycle power plants (CCPP). [BM13] Therefore, besides the simulations
of the cases in the bulleted list above, additional simulations are performed of
all the cases including a DCMD system integrated at the exhaust gas after the
HRSG (flue-gas DCMD).
A simulation for each setup is performed and the GT net power production as
well as the plant electric efficiency is seen in figure 4.6. The GT setups with 1
GT and supplementary firing generally have a low electric efficiency, because the
supplementary firing delivers a large amount of energy, that only the bottoming
cycle benefits from. Compared to the setups with 2 GTs including supplemen-
tary firing (not the Alstom turbine), here the electric efficiency grows, because
the fraction of energy delivered to the system from supplementary firing is less,
hence the majority of the fuel is consumed in the GTs first plus a small addi-
tional amount of energy from the supplementary firing burner is recovered in
the HRSG to suffice the bottoming cycle. This setup yields largest electric effi-
ciencies, because the supplementary firing delivers only a smaller fraction of the
energy. Finally, a general trend can be seen for all three GT models, that when
no supplementary firing is used, the system is over-dimensioned, because the ex-
haust gas energy output is larger than what is necessary to drive the bottoming
cycle, and the electric efficiency drops compared to the previous setup with lit-
tle supplementary firing. It is no surprise that the more GTs are included in a
repowering setup, the larger the net power production from the gas turbine will
be, which can be seen in the clustered columns.
Generally the GT net power production is below the catalogue value as seen
in the column diagram in figure 4.6. This is not because they do not have the
ability to reach the power found in table 4.4, 4.5 and 4.6 for the stand-alone
GT, however it is to compensate for pressure losses in the HRSG. The GT is
run in back-pressure mode and the exhaust flow is extracted before it reaches
==PAGE:84==
66
4 Results
Figure 4.6: The gas turbine net power production and electric efficiency
ambient pressure at 1.013 bar, and rather at pressure levels able to drive the flow
through the HRSG as well. Hence the turbine expands over a smaller pressure
difference giving a reduced net power output, compared to if it were a stand-alone
gas turbine that expands the working fluid until ambient pressure at 1.013 bar.
When the exhaust gas is used for a flue-gas DCMD system, pressure losses in
the flue-gas DCMD heat exchanger must be accounted for as well, and hence the
system produces slightly less than when no flue-gas DCMD system is integrated,
as seen in the clustered columns.
As seen in figure 4.6, a repowering solution with the GE Energy MS7001EA
reaches an electric efficiency high of 42.99 % and a GT net power output of
137.2 MW with 2 GTs including supplementary firing.
If DCMD flue-gas is
integrated in the setup, the efficiency drops slightly to 42.14 % and a GT net
power production of 128.32 MW, because as mentioned previously, the GT runs at
back-pressure and the expansion process is stopped earlier so that the pressure of
the exhaust gas can withstand pressure loss in the flue-gas DCMD heat exchanger.
A repowering solution with the Mitsubishi M501DA GT reaches a maximum
electric efficiency of 44.52 % and a GT net power output of 183.33 MW using 2
GTs including supplementary firing. When the flue-gas DCMD is included, the
electric efficiency drops slightly to 43.63 % and the GT net power output follows
to 172.39 MW.
Finally a repowering solution with the Alstom GT13E2 turbine maximizes electric
efficiency using just one GT including supplementary firing and reaches 45.13 %
and a GT net power output of 168.06 MW. When including flue-gas DCMD it
drops to 44.34 % with a GT net power output of 158.81 MW.
==PAGE:85==
4.2 Gas Turbine Repowering
67
The energy efficiency of the GT repowering simulations can be seen in figure 4.7
along with the stacked net power production of the plant split into the contribu-
tion of the steam cycle and the GT. In a repowering solution, the steam cycle
produces a constant net power output of 117.06 MW - the only auxiliary power
consuming components in the bottoming cycle are the feedwater pump and the
pumps in the DCMD system for the condenser and process heat, which adds up
to 2.57 MW.
Figure 4.7: The net power production and energy efficiency
To properly consider the efficiency of the repowering systems, it is important to
take the energy efficiency into account as well, but it can create confusion and
bias when comparing the systems. The energy efficiency is large in the cases
with 1 GT including a large contribution of supplementary firing, which is the
opposite of the electric efficiency. The solution with 1 GE Energy MS7001EA
GT reaches an energy efficiency of 88.83 %. Using 1 Mitsubishi M501DA GT
gains an energy efficiency of 87.61 % and finally using 1 Alstom GT13E2 turbine
reaches an energy efficiency of 82.28 %.
The energy efficiency may well be large, in such a setup even though the electric
efficiency is low, because the heat energy recovered in the DCMD system in the
process heater and condenser contributes to the majority of the energy return.
Equation 3.7 explains the energy efficiency, and it doesn’t differentiate whether
the output is power or heat. So in repowering schemes where the supplementary
firing contributes to a large fraction of heat, the electric efficiency is low, however
the energy efficiency is high, because a large fraction of the total fuel consumed
is recovered heat in the DCMD system.
The picture is even more biased when the flue-gas DCMD system is coupled to
==PAGE:86==
68
4 Results
the exhaust gas from the HRSG. The energy efficiency including DCMD flue-gas
is also displayed in figure 4.7 in the top graph and it is less volatile to the changes
in system setup. This steady energy efficiency incl. DCMD flue-gas is the result
of the flue-gas DCMD exchanger recovering whatever remaining heat available
until the flue-gas reaches the stack temperature, Tstack = 120.2◦C.
To clarify the influence of the heat recovered in the DCMD systems and how it
influences the energy efficiency, figure 4.8 shows the energy balance of power and
heat recovered in the system setups. In figure 4.8 it is more obvious how large
the contribution of heat recovered is compared to the power generated in each
system setups. The energy efficiency should perhaps not be the main parameter
to consider when choosing repowering system, but instead the electric efficiency.
Figure 4.8: The energy balance displaying the net power output of the GT and
steam cycle as well as the heat recovered distributed over the process heater, the
condenser and the flue-gas heat exchanger
If the goal with repowering is to produce the highest plant net power output,
then the options with many GTs and no supplementary firing are more suitable,
but they yield low electric efficiencies due to over-dimensioning. If the largest
energy efficiency is the goal, then perhaps the solutions with a single GT and
large contributions of supplementary firing yields the highest value. However, the
goal in this case is to repower the bottoming cycle, and simultaneously produce
a larger net power output, but also at a competitive electric efficiency.
The
energy efficiency should be seen as a secondary parameter to measure the system
performance.
Finally, the contribution of the flue-gas DCMD on the energy
efficiency should have the least influence in terms of choosing a proper solution,
because it will, no matter what, recover the remaining energy from the exhaust
gas.
==PAGE:87==
4.2 Gas Turbine Repowering
69
Taking into account that the electric efficiency along with the power output
should be the primary parameters for choosing a solution, the best solutions
for the different gas turbine models are summed up in table 4.8.
The system setup with 2 Mitsubishi M501DA GTs including supplementary firing
is chosen for the gas turbine repowering solution, because it has a larger electric
efficiency, 44.52 %, and net power output, 300.39 MW, than the GE Energy
MS7001EA. The Alstom GT reaches a higher electric efficiency of 45.13 %, how-
ever the flexibility of producing power with 2 gas turbines, leaves the option of
running just one if maintenance is necessary on the other. Finally, the energy
efficiency of the repowering scheme with 2 Mitsubishi M501DA GTs is less than
the other cases, 79.29 % against 82.28 % and 82.66 % respectively. However when
the flue-gas DCMD is taken into account it almost reaches the energy efficiency
of the other solutions, 85.48 % against 86.27 % and 86.34 %.

4.3
SOFC Repowering
In repowering with an SOFC system, no commercial system was taken into ac-
count similar to the gas turbine solution. Instead the system was designed in
order to fit as a solution for repowering the bottoming cycle.
4.3.1
Implementation of Splitter and Hybrid Recuperator
The SOFC topping cycle is first modelled without a splitter after the cathode
preheater as seen in figure 4.9a , however, due to this setup, the off-gas tempera-
ture from the burner, T23, resulted in temperatures below the steam temperature,
T1,steam = 488◦C. The temperature of the off-gas, T23 from the burner entering
the superheater, should be higher than T1,steam = 488◦C. As mentioned ear-
lier, considering a terminal temperature difference, the minimum temperature at
the burner outlet should be T23,minimum = 513◦. [Rok15a] Because the SOFC
topping cycle setup without the splitter produced off gas temperatures below
T23,minimum = 513◦, the setup up was immediately modified and fitted with a
splitter before further simulations. Without the splitter, all the off-air from the
cathode, was directed to the burner. This off-air, ˙m91, had a much larger mass
flow than the off-fuel from the anode, ˙m83, due to the necessary amount of oxidant
involved in the electrochemical reactions in the SOFC. The off-air temperature
T92 was relatively low (after exchanging energy in the cathode preheater to the
fresh air), and the result of this was that large off-air mass flows cooled the burner
temperature below 488◦C.
The three setups involving the off-air from the cathode entering the burner are
seen in figure 4.9. In figure 4.9a the setup includes no splitter. This setup is
disgarded as mentioned above and a splitter is integrated, as seen in figure 4.9b.
Finally, as mentioned in the methods, the hybrid recuperator is implemented for
recovering off air energy, as seen in figure 4.9c.
The function of the splitter lies in the control of the burner temperature. When
a splitter is implemented, the necessary temperature from the burner is adjusted
by the amount of off-air entering the burner.
Initially the SOFC is modelled using parameter values as seen in the methods in
table 3.10. The SOFC model and base case model are integrated, by letting the
flue gas from the burner (node 23) enter the HRSG. The mass flow of natural gas,
˙mNG, is varied along with the number of stacks until the topping cycle is large
enough to run the bottoming cycle. In order for the bottoming cycle to run, the
SOFC should be sized at approximately 400000 stacks with a mass flow of fuel
at ˙mNG = 22kg/s.

4.3.2
Optimizing SOFC Stacks, Utilization Factor and Operating
Temperature
The SOFC topping cycle can be optimized by adjusting different parameters.
The fuel cell stacks, the utilization factor and the operating temperature are
chosen parameters that are optimized for more efficient fuel cell operation. In
the previous simulations the mentioned parameters were set at 400000 stacks,
a fuel utilization factor of Uf = 0.8 remembering to keep the fuel mass flow
constant at 18kg/s and an operating temperature of 780◦C as mentioned in table
3.10.
The first parameter to be varied is the number of stacks. The number of stacks
is considered a factor of fuel cell sizing, because the stack number along with
the oxidant and fuel are size limiting/optimizing factors.[Rok13] The impact of
varying the stacks is seen on the electric efficiency in figure 4.10. The stacks are
increased from 360000 to 440000 stacks, keeping the mass flow of fuel constant.
The electric efficiency grows with the number of stacks, and the trend of the slope
is that it decreases slightly, however still yielding a positive slope. It is important
to mention, that the increase in electric efficiency is only approximately 1% over
the variation of stacks - from 57.72% to 58.67%, hence very small.
Figure 4.10: The SOFC electric efficiency as a function of the number of stacks
==PAGE:92==
74
4 Results
The same trend can be found for the power production of the SOFC, when varying
the stacks between 360000 and 440000 as seen in figure 4.11. The power grows
from 429 MW to 436 MW, however the slope decreases slightly when the number
of stacks are increased.
The electric efficiency and power are both expected
to grow slowly when increasing the number of stacks (if the mass flow is kept
constant and satisfies the amount of stacks), because by increasing the number
of stacks the cell voltage grows.
There is a clear relationship between the fuel flow, oxidant flow and number of
stacks with regards to sizing of the plant, and therefore they should be set to
fit the demanded plant size. Furthermore, the SOFC stacks are directly related
to the cost of the system, therefore the cost will be a boundary condition when
designing an SOFC, in this case it should be sized and designed to be able to
drive the base case steam cycle.[Rok14] [Rok13]
The amount of stacks are kept at 400000 producing a net output of 433.01 MW in
the SOFC and a plant net power production of 508.82 MW at an electric efficiency
of 58.25%. With regards to choosing the amount of stacks for the design it is
important to notice that there is no optimum, hence 400000 stacks are considered
a reasonable amount and will be a set design criteria in further calculations.
Figure 4.11: The SOFC net power production as a function of the number of
stacks
Keeping 400000 stacks constant as a design setting, the utilization factor is now
evaluated by parametrising it between 0.73 and 0.9. The utilization factor is, as
mentioned in equation 2.6, the mass of fuel reacted, divided by the fuel input.
The impact of parametrising the fuel utilization factor is seen, with regards to the
electric efficiency and the plant net power output, in figure 4.12. The net power
output of the plant reaches an optimum at 512.82 MW and an electric efficiency
==PAGE:93==
4.3 SOFC Repowering
75
optimum of 58.71%, at a utilization factor of 0.76. When the utilization factor is
higher than 0.76, too much fuel is input according to the amount of stacks. If too
much fuel is input according to the set amount of stacks, then the concentration
losses are large.
At high concentration losses the cell voltage experiences an
immediate decline.
Figure 4.12: The plant electric efficiency and net power output as a function of
the utilization factor - at 400000 stacks
The cell voltage or operational voltage is seen in figure 4.13 along with the SOFC
power consumption. The cell voltage drops from 0.79V at Uf = 0.73 to 0.47V at
Uf = 0.9. The rapid drop in cell voltage, caused by too much fuel per stack, fuel
crossovering, internal currents, mass transport and concentration losses, confirms
the rapid drop in electric efficiency seen in figure 4.12. Fuel crossovering and
internal currents are the diffusion of fuel and electrons through the electrolyte,
which has a negative impact on the SOFC performance, as ions alone should pass
through the electrolyte in order to react with the oxygen in the cathode side. If
electrons diffuse in the electrolyte, they do not contribute to powering the electric
circuit. [LD03] Furthermore, the power consumption grows significantly for the
SOFC, from 29.4 MW to 85.7 MW, when the fuel utilization factor is increased,
because more air is needed for the electrochemical conversion simultaneously,
hence the compressor work increases.
It should be mentioned that if the number of stacks were modified, by increasing
them, then each stack would receive less fuel, and the optimum fuel utilization
factor would be higher. This iteration, will however not be done, because cost is
linked to the amount of stacks and even though the repowering solutions aren’t
analysed from a technoeconomical point of view, over-dimensioning the system is
avoided. [Rok13] Therefore the fuel utilization factor is kept at 0.76 for further
==PAGE:94==
76
4 Results
simulations with an electric efficiency of 58.71%, a power production of 512.8
MW and a cell voltage of 0.77V.
Figure 4.13: The fuel cell operational voltage and power consumption as a func-
tion of the utilization factor - at 400000 stacks
Finally the operating temperature is analysed, which is a parameter that highly
affects the performance of the fuel cell. The SOFC fuel cell can, based on scientific
assumptions, operate on temperatures down to 500◦C , however in reality low
acheivable operating temperatures are still in the range of 650 −750◦C.[Ste12]
This is of high significance, because fuel cells have high theoretical efficiencies at
lower operating temperatures opposite to the carnot efficiency being highest at
large temperature differences. [Rok15c]
The operating temperature is reduced from 780◦C to 730◦C, and the impact on
the SOFC net power output and the plant electric efficiency is seen in figure
4.14. The efficiency and power output grows when the operating temperature
is lowered, respectively from 58.71% to 68.12% and 395.76 MW to 469.24 MW.
Referring to figure 2.14, it is expected that the fuel cell efficiency grows linearly
with a small slope as the operating temperature is lowered.
The positive impact of lowering the operation temperatures on the electric effi-
ciency is related to the growth in cell voltage and the drop in power consumption
as seen in figure 4.15. With lower operating temperatures, there is a smaller
demand for cooling of the stacks. This leads to a decrease in the mass flow of
the compressed air entering the anode and cathode and therefore a decrease in
the work for the compressors, hence the drop in SOFC power consumption, from
34.05 MW to 20.83 MW.
The anode inlet temperature is set at 130◦C below the operating temperature
and the cathode inlet temperature at 180◦C below the operating temperature.
==PAGE:95==
4.3 SOFC Repowering
77
Figure 4.14: The SOFC power consumption and the operational voltage as a
function of reducing the fuel cell operating temperature - at Uf = 0.76 and
400000 stacks
[Rok13] The CPO reformer carries out an exothermic chemical reaction as men-
tioned earlier, hence it increases the temperature of the natural gas prior to
entering the anode side of the SOFC. This puts a limitation on lowering the op-
erating temperature to 130◦C degrees more than the CPO temp which is set to
525 C. [Rok13]
This however isn’t an issue, because in this case the operating temperature is
lowered to just 730◦C - at temperatures below this, the simulation experiences
convergence issues, because the energy in the off-gas is too low to drive the bot-
toming cycle.
When integrating the SOFC topping cycle with the bottoming cycle, it was nec-
essary to input a condition in the evaporator. A pinch temperature of 10◦C was
input, however when lowering the operating temperature, this condition was a
limitation for the convergence of the simulation and therefore the temperature dif-
ference of 10◦C had to be removed and the additional condition was implemented
at the economizer instead.
Lowering the operating temperature also has its challenges with regards to opti-
mal fuel reforming temperatures be it steam or air reforming, material limitations
in the plates, the thickness of the electrolyte. For instance, with high SOFC oper-
ating temperatures, internal reformation reactions in the fuel cell reform lighter
hydrocarbons e.g. methane to hydrogen and carbon monoxide, which increases
the utilization of fuel and leads to an increase in power production of the SOFC.
When lowering the operating temperatures, it may weaken these internal refor-
mation reactions and thereby decrease performance of the SOFC. However, all in
==PAGE:96==

all, lowering the operating temperature is in this case favourable.[Ste12] [Rok13]

uses steam and needs a source for start-up, however when integrating it with an
SOFC plant it yields higher efficiencies. This will however not be investigated in
this analysis. [Rok13]
4.4
Summary
The base case system including a steam cycle and desalination system is presented
and fitted, and results are given above. In the base case, when the steam cycle
runs on flue-gas from a coal furnace, it produces a net power output of 110.64
MW, with an electric efficiency of 29.88%. It produces an 7.46kg/s of potable
water from the process heat and 66.69kg/s from the condenser. The total potable
water production of 74.15kg/s is produced by recovering 234.68 MJ/s heat. The
total energy efficiency of the bottoming cycle prior to repowering is 93.26%.
Repowering scenarios are presented and discussed above.
Several gas turbine
solutions are described and simulated, and one solution is chosen. A solution
with 2 Mitsubishi M501DA gas turbines including supplementary firing is chosen
for repowering. A flue-gas DCMD system is integrated to recover the remaining
energy from the flue gas released from the HRSG. Finally, the GTs produce a
net power production of 172.39 MW, and adding the steam cycle contribution,
the plant net power output emerges to 289.41 MW. The plant electric efficiency
reaches 43.63% and with the extra water production from the flue-gas DCMD
of 13.58kg/s, the total water production reaches 87.73kg/s and a total energy
efficiency including the flue-gas DCMD system amounts to 85.48%.
Finally the SOFC is analysed for repowering purposes.
It is presented with
and without a hybrid recuperator initially.
The option including the hybrid
recuperator is further optimized with regards to number of stacks, fuel utilization
factor and operating temperature. The optimal SOFC topping cycle was found
at 400000 stacks fuelled 18kg/s, with a fuel utilization factor Uf = 0.76 and an
operating temperature of 730◦C. The SOFC topping cycle alone produces a net
power output of 469.52 MW and the total plant net power output amounts to
==PAGE:98==
80
4 Results
586.58 MW. The electric efficiency reaches a high of 67.16% outmatching the GT
as expected, including the flue-gas DCMD the plant boasts an energy efficiency
of 95.11%.
The energy efficiency isn’t necessarily a good indicator of performance alone as
discussed earlier. A power producing plant’s fuel utilization, which the efficiency
de facto is an indicator of, does not particularly describe the performance of the
plant by itself, because in power producing plants, decreasing power production
slightly, often results in a large increase in heat production. Hence, if a large en-
ergy efficiency was assumed the more viable key indicator for plant performance,
then it would urge a combination of large heat production and small power pro-
duction, because it does not differentiate between heat and power produced. The
impact of a combination of large heat production and small power production
leaves one with a high overall efficiency, hence performing well thermodynami-
cally. However, a thermodynamic indicator may not necessarily be economically
feasible. A better performance indicator would take the cost of heat and power
into account, depending on the fluctuating power market prices. Therefore, the
electric efficiency was used as a primary performance indicator over the secondary
energy efficiency.
A thermo-economic (exergy analysis) as well as a techno-economic evaluation
would give a better understanding of the performance of a solution along with
the thermodynamic indicators such as efficiency. However, a thermo-economic as
well as a techno-economic analysis is considered beyond the scope of this project
and is suitable for further investigation.
==PAGE:99==
CHAPTER 5
Comparison
As mentioned in the results, two repowering solutions were chosen - a GT solution
and an SOFC solution. They will be compared and discussed and evaluated on
several parameters such as the heat transfer in the HRSG, the emissions and
more precisely the specific CO2 emission, which is interesting keeping in mind
that both systems run on natural gas.
The GT solution chosen was powered by 2 Mitsubishi M501DA gas turbines in-
cluding supplementary firing and a flue-gas DCMD system integrated to recover
the remaining energy. The GTs in the topping cycle produced a net power pro-
duction of 172.4 MW, and a plant net power output of 289.4 MW. The plant
electric efficiency reached 43.6% and a total water production of 87.7kg/s. The
total energy efficiency including the flue-gas DCMD system amounted to 85.5%.
The other repowering solution was the SOFC topping cycle with a hybrid recuper-
ator. The fuel cell had 400000 stacks fuelled by a mass flow of 18kg/s, with a fuel
utilization factor Uf = 0.76 and an operating temperature of 730◦C. The SOFC
topping cycle alone produced a net power output of 469.5 MW and the total
plant net power output amounted to 586.6 MW. The electric efficiency reached a
high 67.2% outmatching the GT as expected, and including the flue-gas DCMD
the plant energy efficiency was 95.1% with a total water production of 77.2kg/s.
5.0.1
Heat Transfer in the Heat Recovery Steam Generator
The heat transfer in the HRSG is of significance, because in this component
the concept of repowering is challenged.
Large losses are involved when two
power producing cycles are integrated, but aren’t dimensioned and designed for
combined cycle production prior to commissioning.
The heat transfer can be
displayed in a Q-T diagram. A Q-T diagram shows the temperature as a function
of the transferred heat for the two fluids that are involved in the heat transfer.
In this case it is steam in the steam cycle which recovers heat in the HRSG from
the source, flue-gas from the GTs or off-gas from the SOFC burner.
==PAGE:100==
82
5 Comparison
The heat transfer between the GT topping cycle and the steam cycle is seen in
figure 5.1. A pinch point is inserted as a condition in the evaporator at a temper-
ature difference of approximately ∆Tpinch = 10◦C. Due to the large temperature
values in this heat transfer diagram, it can be hard to identify that there is a
temperature difference located at the pinch point. It is important to incorporate
this pinch temperature, because if a heat exchanger was to exchange heat with
a lower temperature difference, the heat exchanger would have to be designed
with a large heat exchanger area. Put it in another way, with regards to invest-
ment and costs, small pinch temperature differences would lead to large capital
costs.
However, small pinch point temperature differences would also lead to
favourable energy costs regarding heat transfer. Finding the optimal pinch is
in fact a trade-off between the capital costs and energy cost. However, in this
case, a temperature difference at the pinch point of ∆Tpinch = 10◦C is considered
reasonable. [DBK14]
Figure 5.1: Q-T diagram of the heat transfer in the HRSG - GT Repowering
The terminal temperature difference is located between the hot flue-gas entering
the superheater and the hot steam exiting it and it is 143◦C. As mentioned
earlier, the minimum terminal temperature difference should be approximately
25◦C, but because a repowering case is an integration of a new topping cycle with
an existing bottoming cycle, the heat transfer cannot be optimized comparable
==PAGE:101==
5 Comparison
83
to a newly designed combined cycle power plant. [BM13] Therefore the terminal
temperature difference is but a reference point. The temperature of the flue-gas
entering the the HRSG is 631◦C and it exits at 178.39◦C. Using these values the
effectiveness of the HRSG can be calculated using the temperatures and for the
combined cycle plant it is
631◦C −178.39◦C
631◦C −20◦C
= 74.08%
(5.1)
The heat transfer between the SOFC topping cycle off-gas from the burner and
the steam cycle is seen in figure 5.2. A pinch point is in this case inserted as a
condition in the economizer, because this is where the heat transfer between the
steam and off-gas is most constrained. A temperature difference of approximately
∆Tpinch = 10◦C is used again. There are extremely large temperature differences
in the heat transfered between the SOFC off-gas and the steam cycle.
It is
important to mention here, that the mass flow from the SOFC burner is 225.89kg/s
compared to the mass flow in the GT solution, 692.6kg/s. In order for the off-
gas to deliver the necessary enthalpy in the HRSG to drive the steam cycle, the
temperature entering the superheater is very high, 1304.06◦C, to compensate for
the relatively small mass flow.
In the GT solution, the mass flow is 3 times
the amount, hence it can tolerate a much lower temperature delivering the same
enthalpy.
1304.06◦C −156.23◦C
1304.06◦C −20◦C
= 89.39%
(5.2)
Despite the large terminal temperature difference in the SOFC case, the HRSG
effectiveness is still larger, 89.39%, than for the GT case, 74.08%.
It should
be mentioned, that the reason for the large terminal temperature differnce in
the SOFC, is that the operating temperature was optimized earlier.
With a
lower operating temperature in the SOFC, less air is needed (from the cathode
compressor) to cool the stacks, hence less air is delivered to the burner and can
be used as mass flow.
The area between the two curves is a measure of the exergy losses in the heat
exchangers.
If it were an option to optimize the HRSG, this area should be
minimized.
For instance, implementing a dual (or triple) pressure cycle can
minimize the exergetic loss in the HRSG. This analysis is a case of repowering,
and as little change in the bottoming cycle should be implemented as possible,
because otherwise it would increase the cost and if redesigning the bottoming
cycle is included, one would question whether a different repowering strategy
should be considerd. In this case, however, actually 3 pressure levels are available
==PAGE:102==
84
5 Comparison
Figure 5.2: Q-T diagram of the heat transfer in the HRSG - SOFC Repowering
due to the HP, IP and LP. When splitting the process of the heat exchange into
pressure groups of two or three, the heat transfer will take place partially at
higher temperatures - in the high pressure parts - hence pushing the steam curve
up due to the fact that the evaporation temperature is higher at higher pressures.
This will cause better utilization of the heat recovery of the flue-gas. Also, when
dividing the evaporation of the working fluid at certain levels of pressure, the
latent heat absorbed when a phase change occurs will also be divided into smaller
steps, thereby minimizing exergetic losses in the evaporation. [Rok15a]

mass based and for comparability, it is given as the specific CO2 emissions given
as per produced kW/h

Comparing the repowering cases to the base case, the GT repowering solution in-
creases power output by approximately 160%, from 110.6 MW to 289.4 MW, and
increases electric efficiency by about 45%, from 29.9% to 43.6% as expected. As
mentioned earlier, the SOFC outmatches the GT repowering solution on electric
efficiency and power output. Comparing the SOFC to the base case, it increases
power output by over 400%, to 586.6 MW, and electric efficiency increase of over
120%. Furthermore, it has a significantly lower specific CO2 emission, which is
an important factor for repowering purposes. The specific CO2 drops by about
75% from 1.34kgCO2/kW hel to 0.304kgCO2/kW hel comparing the SOFC to the coal
fired base case and the GT specific CO2 emission drops by approximately 65%
from 1.34kgCO2/kW hel to 0.471kgCO2/kW hel. The favourable specific CO2 emission

that the SOFC repowering solution is 67.2% compared to the 43.6% electric effi-
ciency of the GT solution. This is important to remember when discussing the
technologies in general - if the GT solution was compared to an SOFC solution
with approximately the same electric efficiency, then the specific CO2 emission
may favour the SOFCto the same extent. [Rok16]
The potable water production based on the heat recovered, however it isn’t a
parameter to compare the cases based on. It is a mere option to recover heat
an apply it for a certain purpose, in this case potable water production.
It
does however contribute to a large energy efficiency, which is worth mentioning,
because perhaps a specific CO2 emission needs to be evaluated on any energy
==PAGE:104==
86
5 Comparison
consuming production.
If the recovered heat was used for district heating or
district cooling in absorption chillers, likewise it would be a parameter that could
be used for comparability.
As already discussed, the energy efficiency isn’t necessarily a good indicator of
performance alone as it promotes large heat production and low power production
to reach a high energy efficiency.
In order to differentiate between heat and
power produced, a better performance indicator would take the cost of heat and
power into account. Furthermore, thermodynamic indicators should be analysed
alongside a feasibility study or a techno- and thermo-economic analysis.
From an engineering perspective it is also important to discuss operation and
maintenance and upscaling possibilities. The GT solution has the option of pro-
ducing power, even if one of the two GTs need service and maintenance. For
the bottoming cycle to proceed with one GT, a large fraction of the energy sup-
plied should come from the supplementary firing burner. Furthermore, if further
repowering was necessary, an extra GT could be integrated and substitute the
supplementary firing burner. This however would lower the electric efficiency as
mentioned earlier. The SOFC design doesn’t have simple upscaling possibilities
and in cases of maintenance or service, the topping cycle would have to be shut
of.
==PAGE:105==
CHAPTER 6
Further Studies
This short chapter will briefly mention some areas that can be further studied
related to this project.
Assumptions and Parameters
Some of the assumptions and parameters considered in this project, were based
on scientific articles and previous projects in similar fields of study. For instance
parameters on turbomachinery, pumping units, losses in heat and combustion
components all had efficiencies, pressure losses etc. Adjusting these values would
change the outcome of the plant in terms of performance. Assessing the signifi-
cance of adjusting some of these parameters may be interesting, if not as a field
of study, then as a critique of the chosen values.
Fuels
Solid Oxide Fuel Cells have the opportunity to be fuelled with other fuels than
natural gas. Hydrogen is the fuel type mostly affiliated with fuel cells. Methanol,
ethanol, ammonia and syngas from different gasification processes can also be
used.
Hydrogen has benefits regarding the emissions involved in the process, emitting
H2O. Hydrogen, however must be kept at high pressure, which is costly.
Gasification of biomass and waste is also an interesting field of study. Biomass is
in some academic contexts considered carbon neutral. Waste is either combusted
or primarily put in landfills, hence waste gasification has the benefit of being a
resource that is less costly than most fuels, and utilizing waste is an option that
counteracts disposing of it in landfills.
Repowering Solutions
Besides gas turbines and fuel cells as topping cycles, there are other repowering
configurations available. For instance concentrated solar power is a technology
integrated with the steam cycle.
==PAGE:106==
88
6 Further Studies
Furthermore, combustion engines internal as well as external could be integrated
in a repowering configuration. Perhaps not as a full repowering configuration,
but as feed water repowering.
Hybrid Desalination Systems
Certain scientific research in the field of desalination argued the importance of
hybridity in large scale desalination. Several of the dominating desalination tech-
nologies are seen in different hybrid examples, such as integrating a distillation
and thermal process. Reverse osmosis is seen with multi stage flash and multiple
effect distillation. This should have a positive effect on desalination plants in an
industrial context.
Techno- and thermoeconomics
A technoeconomic evaluation is a necessary assessment in energy engineering.
The feasibility of a project is, along with brief modelling or calculation of the ther-
modynamic performance, one of the first evaluations made on a project. With-
out the viability or economic aspect of a project, the realization would not move
forward. Therefore it is a necessary assessment in power plant and energy engi-
neering.
Thermoeconomics is the study of exergy analysis. A thorough exergy analysis
gives an assessment of the available energy, work and the losses, hence it can give
a broader understanding of the performance of a system.
Repowering or Decommissioning
Evaluating whether repowering is feasible at all is only possible by initiating a
technoeconomic analysis. Regarding the efficiency, cost, emissions etc. of repow-
ering a power plant the conclusion would in cases be that the cost of repowering
simply exceeds the gain. Hence, decommissioning and erecting a new plant may
be found to be more feasible for a given plant repowering proposal. A repower-
ing configuration that can compete with new power plants with high efficiencies,
matching new global standards and targets regarding emissions is a task that
requires a full assessment, including a technoeconomic analysis.
==PAGE:107==
CHAPTER 7
Conclusion
The aim of this project was to thermodynamically investigate and compare re-
powering solutions of a defined base case steam cycle integrated with seawater
desalination. The chosen topping cycles were a gas turbine solution and an SOFC
solution and both were fuelled with natural gas for comparability.
Apart from reducing specific emissions, repowering offers increasing the net power
production of a plant and gaining higher electric efficiencies, which was the overall
result of the analysis.
Initially a base case was defined with a steam cycle fuelled with coal integrated
with a direct contact membrane distillation system for seawater desalination.
The base case net power production was 110.64 MW and the desalinated water
production was 74.1 kg/s recovering 234.7 MJ/s heat for the water production.
Furthermore it reached an electric efficiency of 29.9%, an energy efficiency of
93.3% and the specific emission of CO2 amounted to 1.34kgCO2/kW hel.
For the first repowering solution, three different gas turbines were simulated
and analysed and the chosen solution was 2 Mitsubishi M501DA gas turbine
units including supplementary firing and recovering additional exhaust heat from
the flue-gas for additional seawater desalination. This repowering solution was
integrated with the base case and had a plant net power output of 289.4 MW
and a potable water production of 87.7kg/s recovering 277.6 MJ/s heat for water
production. The plant electric efficiency reached 43.6% with an energy efficiency
of 85.5% and a specific emission of 0.471 kgCO2/kW hel.
Finally, an SOFC topping cycle was designed and initially compared with and
without a hybrid recuperator. It was optimized with regards to sizing the amount
of stacks, the fuel utilization factor and modifying operating temperature. The
final SOFC topping cycle integrated with the bottoming cycle produced a plant
net power production of 586.6 MW and 77.2kg/s potable water from 244.2 MJ/s
recovered heat. Furthermore, the electric efficiency reached 67.2%, the overall
energy efficiency was 95.1% and with a specific emission of 0.304kgCO2/kW hel.
The repowering solutions were compared to the base case on several thermody-
namic parameters. It can be concluded that the benefits of repowering are an
==PAGE:108==
90
7 Conclusion
increase in electric efficiency while resulting in a large net increase of power out-
put. The SOFC technology proved to be not just competitive, but to outmatch
the chosen GT repowering solution on several parameters mentioned such as
electric efficiency, net power output and specific CO2 emission. The highly com-
petitive fuel cell technology urges further study in fields of alternative repowering
solutions other than the conventional GT repowering solution.
From a thermodynamic point of view, repowering may not give as good efficien-
cies as a newly designed plant, but from an engineering point of view it stands
as a viable solution to adapting to growing electricity demands while mitigating
CO2 emissions by increasing plant electric efficiencies.
